I.  TREATS OF THE PLACE WHERE OLIVER TWIST WAS BORN AND OF THE CIRCUMSTANCES ATTENDING I.  TREATS OF THE PLACE BIRTH    Among other public buildings in a certain town, which for many reasons it will be prudent to refrain from mentioning, and to which I will assign no fictitious name, there is one anciently common to most towns, great or small: to wit, a workhouse; and in a workhouse was born; on a day and date which I need not trouble myself to repeat, inasmuch as it can be of no possible consequence to the reader, in this stage of the business at all events; the item of mortality whose name is prefixed to the head of this chapter.    For a long time after it was ushered into this world of sorrow and trouble, by the parish surgeon, it remained a matter of considerable doubt whether the child would survive to bear any name at all; in which case it is somewhat more than probable that these memoirs would never have appeared; or, if these memoirs had, that being comprised within a couple of pages, these memoirs would have possessed the inestimable merit of being the most concise and faithful specimen of biography, extant in the literature of any age or country.    Although I am not disposed to maintain that the being born in a workhouse, is in itself the most fortunate and enviable circumstance that can possibly befall a human being, I do mean to say that in this particular instance, it was the best thing for Oliver Twist that could by possibility have occurred. The fact is, that there was considerable difficulty in inducing Oliver to take upon Oliver the office of respiration, a troublesome practice, but one which custom has rendered necessary to our easy existence; and for some time Oliver lay gasping on a little flock mattress, rather unequally poised between this world and the next: the balance being decidedly in favour of the latter. Now, if, during this brief period, Oliver had been surrounded by careful grandmothers, anxious aunts, experienced nurses, and doctors of profound wisdom, Oliver would most inevitably and indubitably have been killed in no time. There being nobody by, however, but a pauper old woman, who was rendered rather misty by an unwonted allowance of beer; and a parish surgeon who did such matters by contract; Oliver and Nature fought out the point between Oliver and Nature. The result was, that, after a few struggles, Oliver breathed, sneezed, and proceeded to advertise to the inmates of the workhouse the fact of a new burden having been imposed upon the parish, by setting up as loud a cry as could reasonably have been expected from a male infant who had not been possessed of that very useful appendage, a voice, for a much longer space of time than three minutes and a quarter.    As Oliver gave this first proof of the free and proper action of Oliver lungs, the patchwork coverlet which was carelessly flung over the iron bedstead, rustled; the pale face of a young woman was raised feebly from the pillow; and a faint voice imperfectly articulated the words, “Let me see the child, and die.”    The surgeon had been sitting with The surgeon turned towards the fire: giving the palms of The surgeon hands a warm and a rub alternately. As the young woman spoke, The surgeon rose, and advancing to the bed head, said, with more kindness than might have been expected of The surgeon:    “Oh, you must not talk about dying yet.”    “Lor bless her dear heart, no!” interposed the nurse, hastily depositing in the nurse pocket a green glass bottle, the contents of which the nurse had been tasting in a corner with evident satisfaction.    “Lor bless the nurse dear heart, when the nurse has lived as long as I have, sir, and had thirteen children of the nurse own, and all on ’em dead except two, and thirteen children of her own, and all on ’em dead except two in the wurkus with me, the nurse’ll know better than to take on in that way, bless the nurse ! Think what it is to be a mother, there a dear young lamb do.”    Apparently this consolatory perspective of a mother prospects failed in producing this consolatory perspective of a mother prospects due effect. The patient shook her head, and stretched out her hand towards the child.    The surgeon deposited the child in her arms. her imprinted her cold white lips passionately on her cold white lips forehead; passed her hands over her face; gazed wildly round; shuddered; fell back and died. They chafed her breast, hands, and temples; but the blood had stopped forever. They talked of hope and comfort. They had been strangers too long.    “It all over, Mrs. Thingummy!” said The surgeon.    “Ah, poor dear, so it is!” said the nurse, picking up the cork of the green bottle, which had fallen out on the pillow, as the nurse stooped to take up the child. “Poor dear!”    “You needn’t mind sending up to me, if the child cries, nurse,” said The surgeon, putting on the child gloves with great deliberation. “It very likely it will be troublesome. Give It a little gruel if It is.” the child put on the child hat, and, pausing by the bed side on the child way to the door, added, “the nurse was a good looking girl, too; where did the nurse come from?”    “the nurse was brought here last night,” replied the nurse, “by the overseer order. the nurse was found lying in the street. the nurse had walked some distance, for the nurse shoes were worn to pieces; but where the nurse came from, or where the nurse was going to, nobody knows.”    The surgeon leaned over the body, and raised the left hand. “The old story,” The surgeon said, shaking The surgeon head: “no wedding ring, I see. Ah! Good night!”    The medical gentleman walked away to dinner; and the nurse, having once more applied The medical gentleman to the green bottle, sat down on a low chair before the fire, and proceeded to dress the infant.    What an excellent example of the power of dress, young Oliver Twist was! Wrapped in the blanket which had hitherto formed young Oliver Twist only covering, young Oliver Twist might have been the child of a nobleman or a beggar; it would have been hard for the haughtiest stranger to have assigned young Oliver Twist young Oliver Twist proper station in society. But now that young Oliver Twist was enveloped in the old calico robes which had grown yellow in the same service, young Oliver Twist was badged and ticketed, and fell into young Oliver Twist place at once a parish child the orphan of a workhouse the humble, half starved drudge to be cuffed and buffeted through the world despised by all, and pitied by none.    Oliver cried lustily. If Oliver could have known that Oliver was an orphan, left to the tender mercies of church wardens and overseers, perhaps Oliver would have cried the louder.  CHAPTER 3
II.  TREATS OF OLIVER TWIST’S GROWTH, EDUCATION, AND BOARD    For the next eight or ten months, OLIVER was the victim of a systematic course of treachery and deception. OLIVER was brought up by hand. The hungry and destitute situation of the infant orphan was duly reported by the workhouse authorities to the workhouse authorities. the workhouse authorities inquired with dignity of the workhouse authorities, whether there was no female then domiciled in “the house” who was in a situation to impart to Oliver Twist, the consolation and nourishment of which OLIVER stood in need. the workhouse authorities replied with humility, that there was not. Upon this, the workhouse authorities magnanimously and humanely resolved, that OLIVER should be “farmed,” or, in other words, that OLIVER should be dispatched to a branch workhouse some three miles off, where twenty or thirty other juvenile offenders against the poor laws, rolled about the floor all day, without the inconvenience of too much food or too much clothing, under the parental superintendence of an elderly female, who received the culprits at and for the consideration of sevenpence halfpenny per small head per week. sevenpence halfpenny per small head per week worth per week is a good round diet for a child; a great deal may be got for sevenpence halfpenny per small head per week, quite enough to overload a great deal stomach, and make sevenpence halfpenny per small head per week uncomfortable. The elderly female was a woman of wisdom and experience; The elderly female knew what was good for children; and The elderly female had a very accurate perception of what was good for The elderly female. So, The elderly female appropriated the greater part of the weekly stipend to The elderly female own use, and consigned the rising parochial generation to even a shorter allowance than was originally provided for the rising parochial generation. Thereby finding in the lowest depth a deeper still; and proving The elderly female a very great experimental philosopher.    Everybody knows the story of another experimental philosopher who had a great theory about a horse being able to live without eating, and who demonstrated a horse so well, that he had got he own horse down to a straw a day, and would unquestionably have rendered he a very spirited and rampacious animal on nothing at all, if he had not died, four and twenty hours before he was to have had he first comfortable bait of air. Unfortunately for the experimental philosophy of the female to whose protecting care Oliver Twist was delivered over, a similar result usually attended the operation of The elderly female system; for at the very moment when the child had contrived to exist upon the smallest possible portion of the weakest possible food, the child did perversely happen in eight and a half cases out of ten, either that the child sickened from want and cold, or fell into the fire from neglect, or got half smothered by accident; in any one of which cases, the miserable little being was usually summoned into another world, and there gathered to the fathers the miserable little being had never known in this.    Occasionally, when there was some more than usually interesting inquest upon a parish child who had been overlooked in turning up a bedstead, or inadvertently scalded to death when there happened to be a washing though the latter accident was very scarce, anything approaching to a washing being of rare occurrence in the farm the jury would take the jury into their heads to ask troublesome questions, or the parishioners would rebelliously affix the parishioners signatures to a remonstrance. But these impertinences were speedily checked by the evidence of the surgeon, and the testimony of the beadle; the former of whom had always opened the body and found nothing inside (which was very probable indeed), and the latter of whom invariably swore whatever the parish wanted; which was very self devotional. Besides, the board made periodical pilgrimages to the farm, and always sent the beadle the day before, to say the beadle were going. the beadle were neat and clean to behold, when the beadle went; and what more would the people have!    It cannot be expected that this system of farming would produce any very extraordinary or luxuriant crop. Oliver Twist ninth birthday found Oliver Twist a pale thin child, somewhat diminutive in stature, and decidedly small in circumference. But nature or inheritance had implanted a good sturdy spirit in Oliver breast. It had had plenty of room to expand, thanks to the spare diet of the establishment; and perhaps to this circumstance may be attributed this circumstance having any ninth birth day at all. Be this as it may, however, it was this circumstance ninth birthday; and this circumstance was keeping it in the coal cellar with a select party of two other young gentleman, who, after participating with this circumstance in a sound thrashing, had been locked up for atrociously presuming to be hungry, when Mrs. Mann, the good lady of the house, was unexpectedly startled by the apparition of Mr. Bumble, the beadle, striving to undo the wicket of the garden gate.    “Goodness gracious! Is that you, Mr. Bumble, sir?” said Mrs. Mann, thrusting Mrs. Mann head out of the window in well affected ecstasies of joy. “(Susan, take Oliver and them two brats upstairs, and wash ’em directly.) My heart alive! Mr. Bumble, sure ly!”    Now, Mr. Bumble was a fat man, and a choleric; so, instead of responding to this open hearted salutation in a kindred spirit, Mr. Bumble gave the little wicket a tremendous shake, and then bestowed upon the little wicket a kick which could have emanated from no leg but a beadle.    “Lor, only think,” said Mrs. Mann, running out, for the three boys had been removed by this time, “only think of that! That I should have forgotten that the gate was bolted on the inside, on account of them dear children! Walk in sir; walk in, pray, Mr. Bumble, do, sir.”    Although this invitation was accompanied with a curtsey that might have softened the heart of a church warden, this invitation by no means mollified a beadle.    “Do you think this respectful or proper conduct, Mrs. Mann,” inquired Mr. Bumble, grasping Mr. Bumble cane, “to keep the parish officers a waiting at your garden gate, when the parish officers come here upon porochial business with the porochial orphans? Are you aweer, Mrs. Mann, that you are, as I may say, a porochial delegate, and a stipendiary?”    “I’m sure Mr. Bumble, that I was only a telling one or two of the dear children as is so fond of Mrs. Mann, that it was you a coming,” replied Mrs. Mann with great humility.    Mr. Bumble had a great idea of Mr. Bumble oratorical powers and Mr. Bumble importance. Mr. Bumble had displayed the one, and vindicated the other. Mr. Bumble relaxed.    “Well, well, Mrs. Mann,” Mr. Bumble replied in a calmer tone; “it may be as you say; it may be. Lead the way in, Mrs. Mann, for I come on business, and have something to say.”    Mrs. Mann ushered the beadle into a small parlour with a brick floor; placed a seat for the beadle; and officiously deposited the beadle cocked hat and cane on the table before the beadle. Mr. Bumble wiped from Mr. Bumble forehead the perspiration which Mr. Bumble walk had engendered, glanced complacently at his cocked hat, and smiled. Yes, Mr. Bumble smiled. Beadles are but men: and Mr. Bumble smiled.    “Now don’t you be offended at what I’m a going to say,” observed Mrs. Mann, with captivating sweetness. “You’m had a long walk, you know, or I wouldn’t mention it. Now, will you take a little drop of somethink, Mr. Bumble?”    “Not a drop. Nor a drop,” said Mr. Bumble, waving Mr. Bumble right hand in a dignified, but placid manner.    “I think you will,” said Mrs. Mann, who had noticed the tone of the refusal, and the gesture that had accompanied the refusal. “Just a leetle drop, with a little cold water, and a lump of sugar.”    Mr. Bumble coughed.    “Now, just a leetle drop,” said Mrs. Mann persuasively.    “What is it?” inquired the beadle.    “Why, it what I’m obliged to keep a little of in the house, to put into the blessed infants’ Daffy, when the blessed infants’ Daffy ain’t well, Mr. Bumble,” replied Mrs. Mann as Mrs. Mann opened a corner cupboard, and took down a bottle and glass. “It gin. I’ll not deceive you, Mr. B. It gin.”    “Do you give the children Daffy, Mrs. Mann?” inquired Mr. Bumble, following with Mr. Bumble eyes the interesting process of mixing.    “Ah, bless ’em, that I do, dear as it is,” replied the nurse. “I couldn’t see ’em suffer before his eyes, you know sir.”    “No”; said Mr. Bumble approvingly; “no, you could not. You are a humane woman, Mrs. Mann.” (Here she set down the glass.) “I shall take a early opportunity of mentioning it to the board, Mrs. Mann.” (Mr. Bumble drew it towards Mr. Bumble.) “You feel as a mother, Mrs. Mann.” (Mr. Bumble stirred the gin and water.) “I I drink your health with cheerfulness, Mrs. Mann”; and Mrs. Mann swallowed half of your health.    “And now about business,” said the beadle, taking out a leathern pocket book. “The child that was half baptized Oliver Twist, is nine year old to day.”    “Bless The child that was half baptized Oliver Twist!” interposed Mrs. Mann, inflaming Mrs. Mann left eye with the corner of Mrs. Mann apron.    “And notwithstanding a offered reward of ten pound, which was afterwards increased to twenty pound. Notwithstanding the most superlative, and, I may say, supernat’ral exertions on the part of this parish,” said Mr. Bumble, ““ have never been able to discover who is Mr. Bumble father, or what was Mr. Bumble mother settlement, name, or condition.”    Mrs. Mann raised Mrs. Mann hands in astonishment; but added, after a moment reflection, “How comes Mr. Bumble to have any name at all, then?”    the beadle drew the beadle up with great pride, and said, “I inwented the beadle.”    “You, Mr. Bumble!”    “I, Mrs. Mann. “ name “ fondlings in alphabetical order. The last was a S, Swubble, I named him. This was a T, Twist, I named him. The next one comes will be Unwin, and the next Vilkins. I have got names ready made to the end of the alphabet, and all the way through it again, when “ come to Z.”    “Why, you’re quite a literary character, sir!” said Mrs. Mann.    “Well, well,” said the beadle, evidently gratified with the compliment; “perhaps I may be. Perhaps I may be, Mrs. Mann.” Mrs. Mann finished the gin and water, and added, Oliver Oliver being now too old to remain here, the board have determined to have Oliver back into the house. I have come out Mrs. Mann to take Oliver there. So let me see Oliver at once.”    “I’ll fetch Oliver directly,” said Mrs. Mann, leaving the room for that purpose. Oliver, having had by this time as much of the outer coat of dirt which encrusted Oliver face and hands, removed, as could be scrubbed off in one washing, was led into the room by Oliver benevolent protectress.    “Make a bow to the gentleman, Oliver,” said Mrs. Mann.    Oliver made a bow, which was divided between the beadle on the chair, and the cocked hat on the table.    “Will you go along with Mrs. Mann, Oliver?” said Mr. Bumble, in a majestic voice.    Oliver was about to say that Oliver would go along with anybody with great readiness, when, glancing upward, Oliver caught sight of Mrs. Mann, who had got behind the chair, and was shaking her fist at Oliver with a furious countenance. Oliver took the hint at once, for her fist had been too often impressed upon Oliver body not to be deeply impressed upon Oliver recollection.    “Will her go with Mrs. Mann?” inquired OliverOliver.    “No, her can’t,” replied Mr. Bumble. “But her’ll come and see you sometimes.”    This was no very great consolation to the child. Young as he was, however, he had sense enough to make a feint of feeling great regret at going away. It was no very difficult matter for the boy to call tears into the boy . Hunger and recent ill usage are great assistants if you want to cry; and Oliver cried very naturally indeed. Mrs. Mann gave Oliver a thousand embraces, and what Oliver wanted a great deal more, a piece of bread and butter, less Oliver should seem too hungry when Oliver got to the workhouse. With the slice of bread in Oliver hand, and the little brown cloth parish cap on Oliver head, Oliver was then led away by Mr. Bumble from the wretched home where one kind word or look had never lighted the gloom of Oliver infant years. And yet Oliver burst into an agony of childish grief, as the cottage gate closed after Oliver. Wretched as were the little companions in misery Oliver was leaving behind, they were the only friends Oliver had ever known; and a sense of Oliver loneliness in the great wide world, sank into the child heart for the first time.    Mr. Bumble walked on with long strides; little Oliver, firmly grasping Oliver gold laced cuff, trotted beside Oliver, inquiring at the end of every quarter of a mile whether they were “nearly there.” To these interrogations Mr. Bumble returned very brief and snappish replies; for the temporary blandness which gin and water awakens in some bosoms had by this time evaporated; and Mr. Bumble was once again a beadle.    Oliver had not been within the walls of the workhouse a quarter of an hour, and had scarcely completed the demolition of a second slice of bread, when Mr. Bumble, who had handed Mr. Bumble over to the care of an old woman, returned; and, telling Mr. Bumble it was a board night, informed Mr. Bumble that the board had said Mr. Bumble was to appear before the board forthwith.    Not having a very clearly defined notion of what a live board was, Oliver was rather astounded by this intelligence, and was not quite certain whether Oliver ought to laugh or cry. Oliver had no time to think about the matter, however; for Mr. Bumble gave Mr. Bumble a tap on the head, with Mr. Bumble cane, to wake Mr. Bumble up: and another on the back to make Mr. Bumble lively: and bidding Mr. Bumble to follow, conducted Mr. Bumble into a large white washed room, where eight or ten fat gentlemen were sitting round a table. At the top of the table, seated in an arm chair rather higher than the rest, was a particularly fat gentleman with a very round, red face.    “Bow to the board,” said Mr. Bumble. Oliver brushed away two or three tears that were lingering in his eyes; and seeing no board but the table, fortunately bowed to that.    “What your name, boy?” said the gentleman in the high chair.    Oliver was frightened at the sight of so many gentlemen, which made Oliver tremble: and the beadle gave Oliver another tap behind, which made Oliver cry. These two causes made Oliver answer in a very low and hesitating voice; whereupon a gentleman in a white waistcoat said Oliver was a fool. Which was a capital way of raising Oliver spirits, and putting Oliver quite at Oliver ease.    “Boy,” said the gentleman in the high chair, “listen to me. You know you’re an orphan, I suppose?”    “What that, sir?” inquired OliverOliver.    “the boy is a fool I thought the boy was,” said a gentleman in a white waistcoata white waistcoat.    “Hush!” said a gentleman in a white waistcoat. “You know you’ve got no father or mother, and that you were brought up by this parish, don’t you?”    “Yes, sir,” replied Oliver, weeping bitterly.    “What are you crying for?” inquired a gentleman in a white waistcoat in a white waistcoat. And to be sure it was very extraordinary. What could the boy be crying for?    “I hope you say your prayers every night,” said another gentleman in a gruff voice; “and pray for the people who feed you, and take care of you like a Christian.”    “Yes, sir,” stammered the boy. The gentleman who spoke last was unconsciously right. It would have been very like a Christian, and a marvellously good Christian too, if Oliver had prayed for the people who fed and took care of Oliver. But Oliver hadn’t, because nobody had taught Oliver.    “Well! You have come here to be educated, and taught a useful trade,” said the red faced gentleman in the high chair.    “So you’ll begin to pick oakum to morrow morning at six o’clock,” added the surly one in a white waistcoat.    For the combination of both these blessings in the one simple process of picking oakum, Oliver bowed low by the direction of the beadle, and was then hurried away to a large ward; where, on a rough, hard bed, Oliver sobbed Oliver to sleep. What a novel illustration of the tender laws of England! They let the paupers go to sleep!    Oliver! Oliver little thought, as Oliver lay sleeping in happy unconsciousness of all around Oliver, that the board had that very day arrived at a decision which would exercise the most material influence over all Oliver future fortunes. But they had. And this was it:    The members of the board were very sage, deep, philosophical men; and when The members of this board came to turn The members of this board attention to the workhouse, The members of this board found out at once, what ordinary folks would never have discovered the poor people liked it! it was a regular place of public entertainment for the poorer classes; a tavern where there was nothing to pay; a public breakfast, dinner, tea, and supper all the year round; a brick and mortar elysium, where it was all play and no work. “Oho!” said the board, looking very knowing; “we are the fellows to set this to rights; we’ll stop it all, in no time.” So, they established the rule, that all poor people should have the alternative (for they would compel nobody, not they), of being starved by a gradual process in the house, or by a quick one out of it. With this view, they contracted with the water works to lay on an unlimited supply of water; and with a corn factor to supply periodically small quantities of oatmeal; and issued three meals of thin gruel a day, with an onion twice a week, and half a roll of Sundays. they made a great many other wise and humane regulations, having reference to the ladies, which it is not necessary to repeat; kindly undertook to divorce poor married people, in consequence of the great expense of a suit in Doctors’ Commons; and, instead of compelling a man to support his family, as they had theretofore done, took his family away from his, and made his a bachelor! There is no saying how many applicants for relief, under these last two heads, might have started up in all classes of society, if it had not been coupled with the workhouse; but the board were long headed men, and had provided for this difficulty. relief was inseparable from the workhouse and the gruel; and that frightened people.    For the first six months after Oliver Twist was removed, the system was in full operation. the system was rather expensive at first, in consequence of the increase in the undertaker bill, and the necessity of taking in the clothes of all the paupers, which fluttered loosely on their wasted, shrunken forms, after a week or two gruel. But the number of workhouse inmates got thin as well as the paupers; and the board were in ecstasies.    The room in which the boys were fed, was a large stone hall, with a copper at one end: out of which the master, dressed in an apron for the purpose, and assisted by one or two women, ladled the gruel at mealtimes. Of this festive composition each boy had one porringer, and no more except on occasions of great public rejoicing, when each boy had two ounces and a quarter of bread besides.    The bowls never wanted washing. the boys polished The bowls with the boys spoons till The bowls shone again; and when The bowls had performed this operation (which never took very long, their spoons being nearly as large as The bowls), The bowls would sit staring at the copper, with such eager eyes, as if The bowls could have devoured the very bricks of which it was composed; employing The bowls, meanwhile, in sucking The bowls fingers most assiduously, with the view of catching up any stray splashes of gruel that might have been cast thereon. Boys have generally excellent appetites. Oliver Twist and Oliver Twist companions suffered the tortures of slow starvation for three months: at last Oliver Twist and his companions got so voracious and wild with hunger, that one boy, who was tall for Oliver Twist age, and hadn’t been used to that sort of thing (for Oliver Twist father had kept a small cook shop), hinted darkly to his companions, that unless Oliver Twist had another basin of gruel per diem, Oliver Twist was afraid Oliver Twist might some night happen to eat the boy who slept next him, who happened to be a weakly youth of tender age. Oliver Twist had a wild, hungry eye; and a wild, hungry eye implicitly believed Oliver Twist. A council was held; lots were cast who should walk up to the master after supper that evening, and ask for more; and it fell to Oliver Twist.    The evening arrived; the boys took the boys places. the master, in the master cook uniform, stationed the master at the copper; the master pauper assistants ranged his pauper assistants behind the master; the gruel was served out; and a long grace was said over the short commons. the gruel disappeared; the boys whispered each other, and winked at Oliver; while Oliver next neighbors nudged the master. Child as Oliver was, Oliver was desperate with hunger, and reckless with misery. Oliver rose from the table; and advancing to the master, basin and spoon in hand, said: somewhat alarmed at Oliver own temerity:    “Please, sir, I want some more.”    the master was a fat, healthy man; but the master turned very pale. the master gazed in stupefied astonishment on the small rebel for some seconds, and then clung for support to the copper. The assistants were paralysed with wonder; the boys with fear.    “What!” said the master at length, in a faint voice.    “Please, sir,” replied Oliver, “I want some more.”    the master aimed a blow at Oliver head with the ladle; pinioned Oliver in Oliver arm; and shrieked aloud for the beadle.    the board were sitting in solemn conclave, when Mr. Bumble rushed into the room in great excitement, and addressing the gentleman in the high chair, said,    “Mr. Limbkins, I beg your pardon, sir! Oliver Twist has asked for more!”    There was a general start. Horror was depicted on every countenance.    “For more!” said Mr. Limbkins. “Compose yourself, Mr. Bumble, and answer me distinctly. Do I understand that he asked for more, after he had eaten the supper allotted by the dietary?”    “he did, sir,” replied Mr. Bumble.    “That boy will be hung,” said a gentleman in a white waistcoata white waistcoat. “I know that That boy will be hung.”    Nobody controverted the prophetic gentleman opinion. An animated discussion took place. Oliver was ordered into instant confinement; and a bill was next morning pasted on the outside of the gate, offering a reward of five pounds to anybody who would take Oliver Twist off the hands of this parish. In other words, five pounds and Oliver Twist were offered to any man or woman who wanted an apprentice to any trade, business, or calling.    “I never was more convinced of anything in my life,” said a gentleman in a white waistcoata white waistcoat, as Oliver knocked at the gate and read a bill next morning: “I never was more convinced of anything in my life, than I am that That boy will come to be hung.”    As I purpose to show in the sequel whether the white waistcoated gentleman was right or not, I should perhaps mar the interest of this narrative (supposing it to possess any at all), if I ventured to hint just yet, whether the life of Oliver Twist had this violent termination or no.  CHAPTER 4
III.  RELATES HOW OLIVER TWIST WAS VERY NEAR GETTING A PLACE WHICH WOULD NOT HAVE BEEN A SINECURE    For a week after the commission of the impious and profane offence of asking for more, Oliver remained a close prisoner in the dark and solitary room to which Oliver had been consigned by the wisdom and mercy of the board. It appears, at first sight not unreasonable to suppose, that, if Oliver had entertained a becoming feeling of respect for the prediction of the gentleman in the white waistcoat, Oliver would have established that sage individual prophetic character, once and for ever, by tying one end of Oliver pocket handkerchief to a hook in the wall, and attaching Oliver to the other. To the performance of this feat, however, there was one obstacle: namely, that pocket handkerchiefs being decided articles of luxury, had been, for all future times and ages, removed from the noses of paupers by the express order of the board, in council assembled: solemnly given and pronounced under their hands and seals. There was a still greater obstacle in Oliver youth and childishness. Oliver only cried bitterly all day; and, when the long, dismal night came on, spread Oliver little hands before Oliver eyes to shut out the darkness, and crouching in the corner, tried to sleep: ever and anon waking with a start and tremble, and drawing Oliver closer and closer to the wall, as if to feel even the wall cold hard surface were a protection in the gloom and loneliness which surrounded Oliver.    Let it not be supposed by the enemies of “the system,” that, during the period of Oliver solitary incarceration, Oliver was denied the benefit of exercise, the pleasure of society, or the advantages of religious consolation. As for exercise, it was nice cold weather, and Oliver was allowed to perform Oliver ablutions every morning under the pump, in a stone yard, in the presence of Mr. Bumble, who prevented Oliver catching cold, and caused a tingling sensation to pervade Oliver frame, by repeated applications of the cane. As for society, Oliver was carried every other day into the hall where the boys dined, and there sociably flogged as a public warning and example. And so far from being denied the advantages of religious consolation, Oliver was kicked into the same apartment every evening at prayer time, and there permitted to listen to, and console Oliver mind with, a general supplication of the boys, containing a special clause, therein inserted by authority of the board, in which they entreated to be made good, virtuous, contented, and obedient, and to be guarded from the sins and vices of Oliver Twist: whom the supplication distinctly set forth to be under the exclusive patronage and protection of the powers of wickedness, and an article direct from the manufactory of the very Devil himself.    It chanced one morning, while Oliver affairs were in this auspicious and comfortable state, that Mr. Gamfield, chimney sweep, went Oliver way down the High Street, deeply cogitating in Oliver mind Oliver ways and means of paying certain arrears of rent, for which Oliver landlord had become rather pressing. Mr. Gamfield most sanguine estimate of Mr. Gamfield finances could not raise most sanguine estimate of his finances within full five pounds of the desired amount; and, in a species of arithmetical desperation, Mr. Gamfield was alternately cudgelling Mr. Gamfield brains and Mr. Gamfield donkey, when passing the workhouse, Mr. Gamfield eyes encountered the bill on the gate.    “Wo o!” said Mr. Gamfield to his donkey.    his donkey was in a state of profound abstraction: wondering, probably, whether his donkey was destined to be regaled with a cabbage stalk or two when his donkey had disposed of the two sacks of soot with which the little cart was laden; so, without noticing the word of command, his donkey jogged onward.    Mr. Gamfield growled a fierce imprecation on his donkey generally, but more particularly on his eyes; and, running after Mr. Gamfield, bestowed a blow on Mr. Gamfield head, which would inevitably have beaten in any skull but a donkey. IV.  OLIVER, BEING OFFERED ANOTHER PLACE, MAKES OLIVER FIRST ENTRY INTO PUBLIC LIFE    In great families, when an advantageous place cannot be obtained, either in possession, reversion, remainder, or expectancy, for the young man who is growing up, it is a very general custom to send the young man who is growing up to sea. The board, in imitation of so wise and salutary an example, took counsel together on the expediency of shipping off Oliver Twist, in some small trading vessel bound to a good unhealthy port. This suggested itself as the very best thing that could possibly be done with him: the probability being, that the skipper would flog him to death, in a playful mood, some day after dinner, or would knock him brains out with an iron bar; both pastimes being, as is pretty generally known, very favourite and common recreations among gentleman of that class. The more the case presented The to The board, in this point of view, the more manifold the advantages of the step appeared; so, they came to the conclusion that the only way of providing for Oliver effectually, was to send Oliver to sea without delay.    Mr. Bumble had been despatched to make various preliminary inquiries, with the view of finding out some captain or other who wanted a cabin boy without any friends; and was returning to the workhouse to communicate the result of Mr. Bumble mission; when Mr. Bumble encountered at the gate, no less a person than Mr. Sowerberry, the parochial undertaker.    Mr. Sowerberry was a tall gaunt, large jointed man, attired in a suit of threadbare black, with darned cotton stockings of the same colour, and shoes to answer. Mr. Sowerberry features were not naturally intended to wear a smiling aspect, but Mr. Sowerberry was in general rather given to professional jocosity. Mr. Sowerberry step was elastic, and Mr. Sowerberry face betokened inward pleasantry, as Mr. Sowerberry advanced to Mr. Bumble, and shook Mr. Bumble cordially by the hand.    “I have taken the measure of the two women that died last night, Mr. Bumble,” said the undertaker.    “You’ll make your fortune, Mr. Sowerberry,” said the beadle, as Mr. Bumble thrust Mr. Bumble thumb and forefinger into the proffered snuff box of the undertaker: which was an ingenious little model of a patent coffin. “I say you’ll make your fortune, Mr. Sowerberry,” repeated Mr. Bumble, tapping the undertaker on the shoulder, in a friendly manner, with Mr. Bumble cane.    “Think so?” said the undertaker in a tone which half admitted and half disputed the probability of the event. “The prices allowed by the board are very small, Mr. Bumble.”    “So are the coffins,” replied the beadle: with precisely as near an approach to a laugh as a great official ought to indulge in.    Mr. Sowerberry was much tickled at this: as of course Mr. Sowerberry ought to be; and laughed a long time without cessation. “Well, well, Mr. Bumble,” Mr. Sowerberry said at length, “there no denying that, since the new system of feeding has come in, the coffins are something narrower and more shallow than the coffins used to be; but we must have some profit, Mr. Bumble. Well seasoned timber is an expensive article, sir; and all the iron handles come, by canal, from Birmingham.”    “Well, well,” said Mr. Bumble, “every trade has every trade drawbacks. A fair profit is, of course, allowable.”    “Of course, of course,” replied the undertaker; “and if I don’t get a profit upon this or that particular article, why, I make a profit up in the long run, you see Mr. Sowerberry! Mr. Sowerberry! Mr. Sowerberry!”    “Just so,” said Mr. Bumble.    “Though I must say,” continued the undertaker, resuming the current of observations which the beadle had interrupted: “though I must say, Mr. Bumble, that I have to contend against one very great disadvantage: which is, that all the stout people go off the quickest. The people who have been better off, and have paid rates for many years, are the first to sink when The people who have been better off, and have paid rates for many years come into the house; and let me tell you, Mr. Bumble, that three or four inches over one calculation makes a great hole in one profits: especially when one has a family to provide for, sir.”    As Mr. Sowerberry said this, with the becoming indignation of an ill used man; and as Mr. Bumble felt that it rather tended to convey a reflection on the honour of the parish; the latter gentleman thought it advisable to change the subject. Oliver Twist being uppermost in Oliver Twist mind, Oliver Twist made Oliver Twist Oliver Twist theme.    “By the bye,” said Mr. Bumble, “you don’t know anybody who wants a boy, do you? A porochial ’prentis, who is at present a dead weight; a millstone, as I may say, round the porochial throat? Liberal terms, Mr. Sowerberry, liberal terms?” As Mr. Bumble spoke, Mr. Bumble raised Mr. Bumble cane to the bill above Mr. Bumble, and gave three distinct raps upon the words “five pounds”: which were printed thereon in Roman capitals of gigantic size.    “Gadso!” said the undertaker: taking Mr. Bumble by the gilt edged lappel of Mr. Bumble official coat; “that just the very thing I wanted to speak to you about. You know dear me, what a very elegant button this is, Mr. Bumble! The board presented this to you on Newyear morning, Mr. Sowerberry. Foolery!” interposed the beadle. “If The board attended to all the nonsense that ignorant jurymen talk, they’d have enough to do.”    “Very true,” said the undertaker; “they would indeed.”    “Juries,” said Mr. Bumble, grasping Mr. Bumble cane tightly, as was Mr. Bumble wont when working into a passion: “juries is ineddicated, vulgar, grovelling wretches.”    “So they are,” said the undertaker.    “they haven’t no more philosophy nor political economy about ’em than that,” said the beadle, snapping the beadle fingers contemptuously.    “No more they have,” acquiesced the undertaker.    “I despise ’em,” said the beadle, growing very red in the face.    “So do I,” rejoined the undertaker.    “And I only wish we’d a jury of the independent sort, in the house for a week or two,” said the beadle; “the rules and regulations of the board would soon bring the rules and regulations of the board spirit down for ’em.”    “Let ’em alone for that,” replied the undertaker. Mr Bumble lifted off Mr Bumble cocked hat; took a handkerchief from the inside of the crown; wiped from Mr Bumble forehead the perspiration which Mr Bumble rage had engendered; fixed the cocked hat on again; and, turning to the undertaker, said in a calmer voice:    “Well; what about the boy?”    “Oh!” replied the undertaker; “why, you know, Mr Bumble, I pay a good deal towards the poor rates.”    “Hem!” said Mr Bumble. “Well?”    “Well,” replied the undertaker, “I was thinking that if I pay so much towards ’em, I’ve a right to get as much out of ’em as I can, Mr. Bumble; and so I think I’ll take the boy.”    Mr Bumble grasped the undertaker by the arm, and led Mr Bumble into the building. Mr. Sowerberry was closeted with the board for five minutes; and the board was arranged that Oliver should go to Mr. Sowerberry that evening “upon liking” a phrase which means, in the case of a parish apprentice, that if the master find, upon a short trial, that the master can get enough work out of a boy without putting too much food into the master, the master shall have the master for a term of years, to do what the master likes with.    When Oliver Oliver was taken before “the gentlemen” that evening; and informed that Oliver was to go, that night, as general house lad to a coffin maker; and that if Oliver complained of Oliver situation, or ever came back to the parish again, Oliver would be sent to sea, there to be drowned, or knocked on the head, as the case might be, Oliver evinced so little emotion, that he was to go, that night, as general house lad to a coffin maker by common consent pronounced Oliver a hardened young rascal, and ordered Mr Bumble to remove Oliver forthwith.    Now, although it was very natural that the board, of all people in the world, should feel in a great state of virtuous astonishment and horror at the smallest tokens of want of feeling on the part of anybody, they were rather out, in this particular instance. The simple fact was, that Oliver, instead of possessing too little feeling, possessed rather too much; and was in a fair way of being reduced, for life, to a state of brutal stupidity and sullenness by the ill usage he had received. he heard the news of he destination, in perfect silence; and, having had he luggage put into he hand which was not very difficult to carry, inasmuch as it was all comprised within the limits of a brown paper parcel, about half a foot square by three inches deep he pulled he cap over he eyes; and once more attaching he to Mr. Bumble coat cuff, was led away by that dignitary to a new scene of suffering.    For some time, Mr Bumble drew Oliver along, without notice or remark; for the beadle carried Mr Bumble head very erect, as a beadle always should: and, it being a windy day, Oliver Oliver was completely enshrouded by the skirts of Mr. Bumble coat as they blew open, and disclosed to great advantage Mr Bumble flapped waistcoat and drab plush knee breeches. Oliver!” said Mr Bumble.    “Yes, sir,” replied Oliver, in a low, tremulous voice.    “Pull that cap off your eyes, and hold up your head, sir.”    Although Oliver did as Oliver was desired, at once; and passed the back of Oliver unoccupied hand briskly across his eyes, Oliver left a tear in Oliver did as he was desired, at once; and passed the back of his unoccupied hand briskly across his eyes when Oliver looked up at Oliver conductor. I wish the Philosopher; could have seen Oliver Twist clutching at the dainty viands that the dog had neglected. Nor were these the only dismal feelings which depressed Oliver. Oliver was alone in a strange place; and we all know how chilled and desolate the best of we will sometimes feel in such a situation. Oliver had no friends to care for, or to care for Oliver. Oliver was awakened in the morning, by a loud kicking at the outside of the shop door: which, before Oliver could huddle on Oliver clothes, was repeated, in an angry and impetuous manner, about twenty five times. When Oliver began to undo the chain, the legs desisted, and a voice began.    “Open the door, will yer?” cried the voice which belonged to the legs which had kicked at the door.    “I will, directly, sir,” replied Oliver: undoing the chain, and turning the key.    “I suppose yer the new boy, ain’t yer?” said the voice through the key hole.    “Yes, sir,” replied Oliver.    “How old are yer?” inquired the voice through the key hole.    “Ten, sir,” replied Oliver.    “Then I’ll whop yer when I get in,” said the voice through the key hole; “you just see if I don’t, that all, my work’us brat!” and having made this obliging promise, the voice through the key hole began to whistle.    Oliver had been too often subjected to the process to which the very expressive monosyllable just recorded bears reference, to entertain the smallest doubt that the owner of the voice through the key hole, whoever Oliver might be, would redeem Oliver pledge, most honourably. Noah was a charity boy, but not a workhouse orphan. No chance child was No chance child, for No chance child could trace No chance child genealogy all the way back to No chance child parents, who lived hard by; No chance child being a washerwoman, and No chance child father a drunken soldier, discharged with a wooden leg, and a diurnal pension of twopence halfpenny and an unstateable fraction. But, now that fortune had cast in fortune way a nameless orphan, at whom even the meanest could point the finger of scorn, fortune retorted on fortune with interest. This affords charming food for contemplation. This shows us what a beautiful thing human nature may be made to be; and how impartially the same amiable qualities are developed in the finest lord and the dirtiest charity boy. They walked on, for some time, through the most crowded and densely inhabited part of the town; and then, striking down a narrow street more dirty and miserable than any They had yet passed through, paused to look for the house which was the object of They search. The houses on either side were high and large, but very old, and tenanted by people of the poorest class: as The houses on either side neglected appearance would have sufficiently denoted, without the concurrent testimony afforded by the squalid looks of the few men and women who, with folded arms and bodies half doubled, occasionally skulked along. A great many of the tenements had shop fronts; but these were fast closed, and mouldering away; only the upper rooms being inhabited. Some houses which had become insecure from age and decay, were prevented from falling into the street, by huge beams of wood reared against the walls, and firmly planted in the road; but even these crazy dens seemed to have been selected as the nightly haunts of some houseless wretches, for many of the rough boards which supplied the place of door and window, were wrenched from their positions, to afford an aperture wide enough for the passage of a human body. The kennel was stagnant and filthy. The very rats, which here and there lay putrefying in The kennel rottenness, were hideous with famine. Mr. Sowerberry and Bumble, being personal friends of the clerk, sat by the vestry room fire with the clerk, and read the paper. VI.  OLIVER, BEING GOADED BY THE TAUNTS OF NOAH, ROUSES INTO ACTION, AND RATHER ASTONISHES HIM    The month trial over, Oliver was formally apprenticed. It was a nice sickly season just at this time. In commercial phrase, coffins were looking up; and, in the course of a few weeks, Oliver acquired a great deal of experience. The success of Mr. Sowerberry ingenious speculation, exceeded even Mr. Sowerberry most sanguine hopes. The oldest inhabitants recollected no period at which measles had been so prevalent, or so fatal to infant existence; and many were the mournful processions which little Oliver headed, in a hat band reaching down to Oliver knees, to the indescribable admiration and emotion of all the mothers in the town. As Oliver accompanied Oliver master in most of Oliver adult expeditions too, in order that Oliver might acquire that equanimity of demeanour and full command of nerve which was essential to a finished undertaker, Oliver had many opportunities of observing the beautiful resignation and fortitude with which some strong minded people bear some strong minded people trials and losses.    For instance; when Sowerberry had an order for the burial of some rich old lady or gentleman, who was surrounded by a great number of nephews and nieces, who had been perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness, and whose grief had been wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasions, an order for the burial of some rich old lady or gentleman, who was surrounded by a great number of nephews and nieces, who had been perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness, and whose grief had been wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasions would be as happy among an order for the burial of some rich old lady or gentleman, who was surrounded by a great number of nephews and nieces, who had been perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness, and whose grief had been wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasions as need be quite cheerful and contented conversing together with as much freedom and gaiety, as if nothing whatever had happened to disturb an order for the burial of some rich old lady or gentleman, who was surrounded by a great number of nephews and nieces, who had been perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness, and whose grief had been wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasions. Husbands, too, bore the loss of Husbands wives with the most heroic calmness. Wives, again, put on weeds for Wives husbands, as if, so far from grieving in the garb of sorrow, Wives had made up Wives minds to render it as becoming and attractive as possible. It was observable, too, that ladies and gentlemen who were in passions of anguish during the ceremony of interment, recovered almost as soon as Wives reached home, and became quite composed before the tea drinking was over. All this was very pleasant and improving to see; and Oliver beheld All this with great admiration.    That Oliver Twist was moved to resignation by the example of these good people, I cannot, although I am Oliver Twist biographer, undertake to affirm with any degree of confidence; but I can most distinctly say, that for many months Oliver Twist continued meekly to submit to the domination and ill treatment of Noah Claypole: who used Oliver Twist far worse than before, now that Oliver Twist jealousy was roused by seeing the new boy promoted to the black stick and hatband, while Oliver Twist, the old one, remained stationary in the muffin cap and leathers. Charlotte treated Oliver Twist ill, because Noah did; and Mrs. Sowerberry was Oliver Twist decided enemy, because Mrs. Sowerberry was disposed to be Mrs. Sowerberry friend; so, between these three on one side, and a glut of funerals on the other, Oliver was not altogether as comfortable as the hungry pig was, when Oliver was shut up, by mistake, in the grain department of a brewery.    And now, I come to a very important passage in Oliver history; for I have to record an act, slight and unimportant perhaps in appearance, but which indirectly produced a material change in all Oliver future prospects and proceedings.    One day, Oliver and Noah had descended into the kitchen at the usual dinner hour, to banquet upon a small joint of mutton a pound and a half of the worst end of the neck when Charlotte being called out of the way, there ensued a brief interval of time, which Noah Claypole, being hungry and vicious, considered Oliver could not possibly devote to a worthier purpose than aggravating and tantalising young Oliver Twist.    Intent upon this innocent amusement, Noah put Noah feet on the table cloth; and pulled Oliver hair; and twitched Oliver ears; and expressed Oliver opinion that Oliver was a “sneak”; and furthermore announced Oliver intention of coming to see Oliver hanged, whenever that desirable event should take place; and entered upon various topics of petty annoyance, like a malicious and ill conditioned charity boy as Oliver was. But, making Oliver cry, Noah attempted to be more facetious still; and in Noah attempt, did what many sometimes do to this day, when they want to be funny. Noah got rather personal.    “Work’us,” said Noah, “how your mother?”    “how your mother?”     dead,” replied Oliver; “don’t you say anything about how your mother?”     to how your mother?”    !”    Oliver colour rose as Oliver said this; Oliver breathed quickly; and there was a curious working of the mouth and nostrils, which Mr. Claypole thought must be the immediate precursor of a violent fit of crying. Under this impression Oliver returned to the charge.    “What did how your mother?”     die of, Work’us?” said Noah.    “Of a broken heart, some of our old nurses told me,” replied Oliver: more as if Oliver were talking to Oliver, than answering Noah. “I think I know what it must be to die of that!”    “Tol de rol lol lol, right fol lairy, Work’us,” said Noah, as a tear rolled down Oliver cheek. “What set you a snivelling now?”    “Not you,” replied Oliver, sharply. “There; that enough. Don’t say anything more to how your mother?”     about how your mother?”    ; you’d better not!”    “Better not!” exclaimed Noah. “Well! Better not! Work’us, don’t be impudent. Your mother, too! how your mother?”     was a nice ’un how your mother?”     was. Oh, Lor!” And here, Noah nodded Noah head expressively; and curled up as much of Noah small red nose as muscular action could collect together, for the occasion.    “Yer know, Work’us,” continued Noah, emboldened by Oliver silence, and speaking in a jeering tone of affected pity: of all tones the most annoying: “Yer know, Work’us, Work’us can’t be helped now; and of course yer couldn’t help Work’us then; and I am very sorry for Work’us; and I’m sure we all are, and pity yer very much. But Yer must know, Work’us, yer mother was a regular right down bad ’un.”    “What did you say?” inquired Oliver, looking up very quickly.    “A regular right down bad ’un, Work’us,” replied Noah, coolly. “And it a great deal better, Work’us, that she died when she did, or else she’d have been hard labouring in Bridewell, or transported, or hung; which is more likely than either, isn’t it?”    Crimson with fury, Oliver started up; overthrew the chair and table; seized Noah by the throat; shook Oliver, in the violence of Oliver rage, till Oliver teeth chattered in Oliver head; and collecting Oliver whole force into one heavy blow, felled Oliver to the ground.    A minute ago, Oliver had looked the quiet child, mild, dejected creature that harsh treatment had made Oliver. But Oliver spirit was roused at last; the cruel insult to Oliver dead mother had set Oliver blood on fire. VIII.  OLIVER WALKS TO LONDON. OLIVER ENCOUNTERS ON THE ROAD A STRANGE SORT OF YOUNG GENTLEMAN    OLIVER reached the stile at which the by path terminated; and once more gained THE ROAD. There were a good many small shops; but the only stock in trade appeared to be heaps of children, who, even at that time of night, were crawling in and out at the doors, or screaming from the inside. Covered ways and yards, which here and there diverged from the main street, disclosed little knots of houses, where drunken men and women were positively wallowing in filth; and from several of the door ways, great ill looking fellows were cautiously emerging, bound, to all appearance, on no very well disposed or harmless errands. Although Oliver had roused Oliver from sleep, Oliver was not thoroughly awake. There is a drowsy state, between sleeping and waking, when you dream more in five minutes with your eyes half open, and yourself half conscious of everything that is passing around you, than you would in five nights with your eyes fast closed, and your senses wrapt in perfect unconsciousness. At such time, a mortal knows just enough of what Oliver mind is doing, to form some glimmering conception of its mighty powers, its bounding from earth and spurning time and space, when freed from the restraint of its corporeal associate.    Oliver was precisely in this condition. XII.  IN WHICH OLIVER IS TAKEN BETTER CARE OF THAN XII.   EVER WAS BEFORE. AND IN WHICH THE NARRATIVE REVERTS TO THE MERRY OLD GENTLEMAN AND XII.   YOUTHFUL FRIENDS.    The coach rattled away, over nearly the same ground as that which Oliver had traversed when Oliver first entered London in company with the Dodger; and, turning a different way when it reached the Angel at Islington, stopped at length before a neat house, in a quiet shady street near Pentonville. But, for many days, Oliver remained insensible to all the goodness of Oliver new friends. The sun rose and sank, and rose and sank again, and many times after that; and still Oliver lay stretched on a bed, dwindling away beneath the dry and wasting heat of fever. The worm does not work more surely on the dead body, than does this slow creeping fire upon the living frame.    Weak, and thin, and pallid, Oliver awoke at last from what seemed to have been a long and troubled dream. A weakness on Oliver part, which affords the narrative an opportunity of relieving the reader from suspense, in behalf of the two young pupils of the Merry Old Gentleman; and of recording     That when the Dodger, and Oliver accomplished friend Master Bates, joined in the hue and cry which was raised at Oliver heels, in consequence of when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates executing an illegal conveyance of Mr. Brownlow personal property, as has been already described, when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates were actuated by a very laudable and becoming regard for when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates; and forasmuch as the freedom of the subject and the liberty of the individual are among the first and proudest boasts of a true hearted Englishman, so, I need hardly beg the reader to observe, that this action should tend to exalt when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates in the opinion of all public and patriotic men, in almost as great a degree as this strong proof of when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates anxiety for when the Dodger, and his accomplished friend Master Bates own preservation and safety goes to corroborate and confirm the little code of laws which certain profound and sound judging philosophers have laid down as the main springs of all Nature deeds and actions: the said philosophers very wisely reducing the good lady proceedings to matters of maxim and theory: and, by a very neat and pretty compliment to her exalted wisdom and understanding, putting entirely out of sight any considerations of heart, or generous impulse and feeling. For, these are matters totally beneath a female who is acknowledged by universal admission to be far above the numerous little foibles and weaknesses of her sex.    If I wanted any further proof of the strictly philosophical nature of the conduct of these young gentlemen in these young gentlemen very delicate predicament, I should at once find it in the fact (also recorded in a foregoing part of this narrative), of these young gentlemen quitting the pursuit, when the general attention was fixed upon Oliver; and making immediately for these young gentlemen home by the shortest possible cut. Although I do not mean to assert that it is usually the practice of renowned and learned sages, to shorten the road to any great conclusion (the practice of renowned and learned sages course indeed being rather to lengthen the distance, by various circumlocutions and discursive staggerings, like unto those in which drunken men under the pressure of a too mighty flow of ideas, are prone to indulge); still, I do mean to say, and do say distinctly, that it is the invariable practice of many mighty philosophers, in carrying out many mighty philosophers theories, to evince great wisdom and foresight in providing against every possible contingency which can be supposed at all likely to affect many mighty philosophers. Thus, to do a great right, you may do a little wrong; and you may take any means which the end to be attained, will justify; the amount of the right, or the amount of the wrong, or indeed the distinction between the two, being left entirely to the philosopher concerned, to be settled and determined by his clear, comprehensive, and impartial view of his own particular case.    It was not until the two boys had scoured, with great rapidity, through a most intricate maze of narrow streets and courts, that the two boys ventured to halt beneath a low and dark archway. Everything was so quiet, and neat, and orderly; everybody so kind and gentle; that after the noise and turbulence in the midst of which Oliver had always lived, it seemed like Heaven itself. Oliver was no sooner strong enough to put Oliver clothes on, properly, than Mr. Brownlow caused a complete new suit, and a new cap, and a new pair of shoes, to be provided for Oliver. As Oliver was told that Oliver might do what Oliver liked with his clothes, Oliver gave his clothes to a servant who had been very kind to Oliver, and asked a servant who had been very kind to him to sell his clothes to a Jew, and keep the money for a servant who had been very kind to him. This a servant who had been very kind to him very readily did; and, as Oliver looked out of the parlour window, and saw a Jew roll his clothes up in Jew bag and walk away, Jew felt quite delighted to think that his clothes were safely gone, and that there was now no possible danger of Jew ever being able to wear his clothes again. his clothes were sad rags, to tell the truth; and Oliver had never had a new suit before.    One evening, about a week after the affair of the picture, as Oliver was sitting talking to Mrs. Bedwin, there came a message down from Mr. Brownlow, that if Oliver Twist felt pretty well, Oliver Twist should like to see Oliver Twist in Oliver Twist study, and talk to Oliver Twist a little while.    “Bless us, and save us! Wash your hands, and let me part your hair nicely for you, child,” said Mrs. Bedwin. “Dear heart alive! When Mr. Brownlow saw Oliver, Mr. Brownlow pushed the book away from Oliver, and told Oliver to come near a table drawn up before the window, at which Mr. Brownlow was seated reading, and sit down. Oliver complied; marvelling where the people could be found to read such a great number of books as seemed to be written to make the world wiser. Which is still a marvel to more experienced people than Oliver Twist, every day of the people lives.    “There are a good many books, are there not, my boy?” said Mr. Brownlow, observing the curiosity with which Oliver surveyed the shelves that reached from the floor to the ceiling.    “A great number, sir,” replied Oliver. “I never saw so many.”    “You shall read them, if you behave well,” said the old gentleman kindly; “and you will like that, better than looking at the outsides, that is, some cases; because there are books of which the backs and covers are by far the best parts.”    “I suppose they are those heavy ones, sir,” said Oliver, pointing to some large quartos, with a good deal of gilding about the binding.    “Not always those,” said the old gentleman, patting Oliver on the head, and smiling as Oliver did so; “there are other equally heavy ones, though of a much smaller size. How should you like to grow up a clever man, and write books, eh?”    “I think I would rather read them, sir,” replied Oliver.    “What! wouldn’t you like to be a book writer?” said the old gentleman.    Oliver considered a little while; and at last said, Oliver should think it would be a much better thing to be a book seller; upon which the old gentleman laughed heartily, and declared Oliver had said a very good thing. Which Oliver felt glad to have done, though Oliver by no means knew what it was.    “Well, well,” said the old gentleman, composing the old gentleman features. “Don’t be afraid! We won’t make an author of you, while there an honest trade to be learnt, or brick making to turn to.”    “Thank you, sir,” said Oliver. The persons on whom I have bestowed my dearest love, lie deep in whom graves; but, although the happiness and delight of my life lie buried there too, I have not made a coffin of my heart, and sealed a coffin of my heart up, forever, on my best affections. Deep affliction has but strengthened and refined Deep affliction.”    As the old gentleman said this in a low voice: more to the old gentleman than to the old gentleman companion: and as the old gentleman remained silent for a short time afterwards: Oliver sat quite still.    “Well, well!” said the old gentleman at length, in a more cheerful tone, “I only say this, because you have a young heart; and knowing that I have suffered great pain and sorrow, you will be more careful, perhaps, not to wound me again. It is worthy of remark, as illustrating the importance we attach to we own judgments, and the pride with which we put forth we most rash and hasty conclusions, that, although Mr. Grimwig was not by any means a bad hearted man, and though Mr. Grimwig would have been unfeignedly sorry to see his singular friend duped and deceived, Mr. Grimwig really did most earnestly and strongly hope at that moment, that Oliver Twist might not come back. XV.  SHOWING HOW VERY FOND OF OLIVER TWIST, THE MERRY OLD JEW AND MISS NANCY WERE    In the obscure parlour of a low public house, in the filthiest part of Little Saffron Hill; a dark and gloomy den, where a flaring gas light burnt all day in the winter time; and where no ray of sun ever shone in the summer: there sat, brooding over a little pewter measure and a small glass, strongly impregnated with the smell of liquor, a man in a velveteen coat, drab shorts, half boots and stockings, whom even by that dim light no experienced agent of the police would have hesitated to recognise as Mr. William Sikes. At Mr. William Sikes feet, sat a white coated, red eyed dog; who occupied Mr. William Sikes, alternately, in winking at Mr. William Sikes master with both eyes at the same time; and in licking a large, fresh cut on one side of Mr. William Sikes mouth, which appeared to be the result of some recent conflict.    “Keep quiet, you warmint! Keep quiet!” said Mr. William Sikes, suddenly breaking silence. Whether Mr. William Sikes meditations were so intense as to be disturbed by the dog winking, or whether Mr. William Sikes feelings were so wrought upon by Mr. William Sikes reflections that they required all the relief derivable from kicking an unoffending animal to allay they, is matter for argument and consideration. Whatever was the cause, the effect was a kick and a curse, bestowed upon the dog simultaneously.    Dogs are not generally apt to revenge injuries inflicted upon Dogs by Dogs masters; but Mr. Sikes dog, having faults of temper in common with his owner, and labouring, perhaps, at this moment, under a powerful sense of injury, made no more ado but at once fixed his teeth in one of the half boots. Having given in a hearty shake, his retired, growling, under a form; just escaping the pewter measure which Mr. Sikes levelled at Mr. Sikes head.    “You would, would you?” said Mr. Sikes, seizing the poker in one hand, and deliberately opening with the other a large clasp knife, which Mr. Sikes drew from Mr. Sikes pocket. “Come here, you born devil! Come here! D’ye hear?”    The dog no doubt heard; because Mr. Sikes spoke in the very harshest key of a very harsh voice; but, appearing to entertain some unaccountable objection to having Mr. Sikes throat cut, Mr. Sikes remained where Mr. Sikes was, and growled more fiercely than before: at the same time grasping the end of the poker between Mr. Sikes , and biting at it like a wild beast.    This resistance only infuriated Mr. Sikes the more; who, dropping on Mr. Sikes knees, began to assail the animal most furiously. The dog jumped from right to left, and from left to right; snapping, growling, and barking; Mr. Sikes thrust and swore, and struck and blasphemed; and the struggle was reaching a most critical point for one or other; when, the door suddenly opening, The dog darted out: leaving Mr. Sikes with the poker and the clasp knife in Mr. Sikes hands.    There must always be two parties to a quarrel, says the old adage. Mr. Sikes, being disappointed of the dog participation, at once transferred his share in a quarrel to the new comer.    “What the devil do you come in between me and The dog for?” said Sikes, with a fierce gesture.    “I didn’t know, my dear, I didn’t know,” replied Fagin, humbly; for the Jew was the new comer.    “Didn’t know, you white livered thief!” growled Mr. Sikes. “Couldn’t you hear the noise?”    “Not a sound of it, as I’m a living man, Bill,” replied the Jew.    “Oh no! You hear nothing, you don’t,” retorted Mr. Sikes with a fierce sneer. “Sneaking in and out, so as nobody hears how you come or go! I wish you had been the dog, Fagin, half a minute ago.”    “Why?” inquired the Jew with a forced smile.    “Cause the government, as cares for the lives of such men as you, as haven’t half the pluck of curs, lets a man kill a dog how a man likes,” replied Mr. Sikes, shutting up the knife with a very expressive look; “that why.”    the Jew rubbed the Jew hands; and, sitting down at the table, affected to laugh at the pleasantry of the Jew friend. the Jew was obviously very ill at ease, however.    “Grin away,” said Mr. Sikes, replacing the poker, and surveying Mr. Sikes with savage contempt; “grin away. All safe!” As he spoke, he drew forth an old cotton handkerchief from he breast; and untying a large knot in one corner, produced a small brown paper packet. Darkness had set in; it was a low neighborhood; no help was near; resistance was useless. In another moment the man was dragged into a labyrinth of dark narrow courts, and was forced along a labyrinth of dark narrow courts at a pace which rendered the few cries the man dared to give utterance to, unintelligible. The gas lamps were lighted; Mrs. Bedwin was waiting anxiously at the open door; the servant had run up the street twenty times to see if there were any traces of Oliver; and still the two old gentlemen sat, perseveringly, in the dark parlour, with the watch between the two old gentlemen. Arter I was locked up for the night, the row and din outside made the thundering old jail so silent, that I could almost have beat my brains out against the iron plates of the door.”    “Poor fellow!” said Nancy, who still had her face turned towards the quarter in which a deep church bell had sounded. “Oh, Bill, such fine young chaps as them!”    “Yes; that all you women think of,” answered Sikes. “Fine young chaps!
