Come on, and don’t stand preaching there.”    the girl burst into a laugh; drew the girl shawl more closely round the girl; and her shawl walked away. But Oliver felt the girl hand tremble, and, looking up in the girl face as her shawl passed a gas lamp, saw that a gas lamp had turned a deadly white.    her shawl walked on, by little frequented and dirty ways, for a full half hour: meeting very few people, and those appearing from her shawl looks to hold much the same position in society as Sikes. At length her shawl turned into a very filthy narrow street, nearly full of old clothes shops; the dog running forward, as if conscious that there was no further occasion for his keeping on guard, stopped before the door of a shop that was closed and apparently untenanted; the house was in a ruinous condition, and on the door was nailed a board, intimating that it was to let: which looked as if a board had hung there for many years.    “All right,” cried Sikes, glancing cautiously about.    Nancy stooped below the shutters, and Oliver heard the sound of a bell. They crossed to the opposite side of the street, and stood for a few moments under a lamp. A noise, as if a sash window were gently raised, was heard; and soon afterwards the door softly opened. Sikes then seized the terrified boy by the collar with very little ceremony; and all three were quickly inside the house.    The passage was perfectly dark. They waited, while the person who had let They in, chained and barred the door.    “Anybody here?” inquired Sikes.    “No,” replied a voice, which Oliver thought Oliver had heard before.    “Is the old ’un here?” asked the robber.    “Yes,” replied the voice, “and precious down in the mouth Oliver has been. Won’t Oliver be glad to see you? Oh, no!”    The style of this reply, as well as the voice which delivered it, seemed familiar to Oliver ears: but it was impossible to distinguish even the form of the speaker in the darkness.    “Let have a glim,” said Sikes, “or we shall go breaking we necks, or treading on the dog. Look after your legs if you do!”    “Stand still a moment, and I’ll get you one,” replied the voice. The receding footsteps of the speaker were heard; and, in another minute, the form of Mr. John Dawkins, otherwise the Artful Dodger, appeared. Mr. John Dawkins bore in Mr. John Dawkins right hand a tallow candle stuck in the end of a cleft stick.    The young gentleman did not stop to bestow any other mark of recognition upon Oliver than a humourous grin; but, turning away, beckoned the visitors to follow Oliver down a flight of stairs. They crossed an empty kitchen; and, opening the door of a low earthy smelling room, which seemed to have been built in a small back yard, were received with a shout of laughter.    “Oh, my wig, my wig!” cried Master Charles Bates, from whose lungs the laughter had proceeded: “here Oliver is! oh, cry, here Oliver is! Oh, Fagin, look at Oliver! Fagin, do look at Oliver! I can’t bear it; it is such a jolly game, I can’t bear it. Hold me, somebody, while I laugh it out.”    With this irrepressible ebullition of mirth, Master Charles Bates laid Master Charles Bates flat on the floor: and kicked convulsively for five minutes, in an ectasy of facetious joy. Then jumping to Master Charles Bates feet, Master Charles Bates snatched the cleft stick from the Dodger; and, advancing to Oliver, viewed Master Charles Bates round and round; while the Jew, taking off Master Charles Bates nightcap, made a great number of low bows to the bewildered boy. The Artful, meantime, who was of a rather saturnine disposition, and seldom gave way to merriment when it interfered with business, rifled Oliver pockets with steady assiduity.    “Look at his togs, Fagin!” said Charley, putting the light so close to his new jacket as nearly to set his on fire. “Look at his togs! Superfine cloth, and the heavy swell cut! Oh, my eye, what a game! And his books, too! Nothing but a gentleman, Fagin!”    “Delighted to see you looking so well, my dear,” said the Jew, bowing with mock humility. “The Artful shall give you another suit, my dear, for fear you should spoil that Sunday one. Why didn’t you write, my dear, and say you were coming? We’d have got something warm for supper.”    At his, Master Bates roared again: so loud, that Fagin relaxed, and even the Dodger smiled; but as the Artful drew forth the five pound note at that instant, it is doubtful whether the sally of the discovery awakened his merriment.    “Hallo, what that?” inquired Sikes, stepping forward as the Jew seized the note. “That mine, Fagin.”    “No, no, my dear,” said the Jew. “Mine, Bill, mine. You shall have the books.”    “If that ain’t mine!” said Sikes, putting on Sikes hat with a determined air; “mine and Nancy that is; I’ll take the terrified boy back again.”    the Jew started. Oliver started too, though from a very different cause; for Oliver hoped that the dispute might really end in Oliver being taken back.    “Come! Hand over, will you?” said Sikes.    “This is hardly fair, Bill; hardly fair, is it, Nancy?” inquired the Jew.    “Fair, or not fair,” retorted Sikes, “hand over, I tell you! Do you think Nancy and me has got nothing else to do with Nancy and me precious time but to spend it in scouting arter, and kidnapping, every young boy as gets grabbed through you? Give it here, you avaricious old skeleton, give it here!”    With this gentle remonstrance, Sikes plucked the note from between the Jew finger and thumb; and looking the old man coolly in the face, folded the note up small, and tied the note in the old man neckerchief.    “That for Nancy and me share of the trouble,” said Sikes; “and not half enough, neither. You may keep the books, if you’re fond of reading. If you ain’t, sell ’em.”    “They’re very pretty,” said Charley Bates: who, with sundry grimaces, had been affecting to read one of the volumes in question; “beautiful writing, isn’t is, Oliver?” At sight of the dismayed look with which Oliver regarded Oliver tormentors, Master Bates, who was blessed with a lively sense of the ludicrous, fell into another ectasy, more boisterous than the first.    “They belong to the old gentleman,” said Oliver, wringing Oliver hands; “to the good, kind, old gentleman who took me into Oliver house, and had me nursed, when I was near dying of the fever. Oh, pray send them back; send Oliver back the books and money. Keep me here all my life long; but pray, pray send them back. Oliver’ll think I stole them; the old lady: all of them who were so kind to me: will think I stole them. Oh, do have mercy upon me, and send them back!”    With these words, which were uttered with all the energy of passionate grief, Oliver fell upon Oliver knees at his feet; and beat his hands together, in perfect desperation.    “The boy right,” remarked Fagin, looking covertly round, and knitting The boy shaggy eyebrows into a hard knot. “You’re right, Oliver, you’re right; they will think you have stolen ’em. Ha! ha!” chuckled the Jew, rubbing The boy , “it couldn’t have happened better, if we had chosen we time!”    “Of course it couldn’t,” replied Sikes; “I know’d that, directly I see The boy coming through Clerkenwell, with the books under The boy arm. It all right enough. They’re soft hearted psalm singers, or They wouldn’t have taken The boy in at all; and They’ll ask no questions after The boy, fear They should be obliged to prosecute, and so get The boy lagged. The boy safe enough.”    Oliver had looked from one to the other, while these words were being spoken, as if Oliver were bewildered, and could scarecely understand what passed; but when Sikes concluded, Sikes jumped suddenly to his feet, and tore wildly from the room: uttering shrieks for help, which made the bare old house echo to the roof.    “Keep back the dog, Bill!” cried Nancy, springing before the door, and closing the door, as the Jew and Jew two pupils darted out in pursuit. “Keep back the dog; Sikes’ll tear The boy to pieces.”    “Serve Sikes right!” cried Sikes, struggling to disengage Sikes from the girl grasp. “Stand off from Sikes, or I’ll split your head against the wall.”    “I don’t care for that, Bill, I don’t care for that,” screamed the girl, struggling violently with Bill, “the child shan’t be torn down by the dog, unless you kill me first.”    “Shan’t Sikes!” said Sikes, setting Sikes teeth. “I’ll soon do that, if you don’t keep off.”    The housebreaker flung the girl from The housebreaker to the further end of the room, just as the Jew and the two boys returned, dragging Oliver among the Jew and the two boys.    “What the matter here!” said Fagin, looking round.    “the girl gone mad, I think,” replied Sikes, savagely.    “No, the girl hasn’t,” said Nancy, pale and breathless from the scuffle; “no, the girl hasn’t, Fagin; don’t think it.”    “Then keep quiet, will you?” said the Jew, with a threatening look.    “No, I won’t do that, neither,” replied Nancy, speaking very loud. “Come! What do you think of that?”    Fagin was sufficiently well acquainted with the manners and customs of that particular species of humanity to which Nancy belonged, to feel tolerably certain that it would be rather unsafe to prolong any conversation with Nancy, at present. With the view of diverting the attention of the company, Fagin turned to Oliver.    “So you wanted to get away, my dear, did you?” said the Jew, taking up a jagged and knotted club which lay in a corner of the fireplace; “eh?”    Oliver made no reply. But Oliver watched the the Jew motions, and breathed quickly.    “Wanted to get assistance; called for the police; did you?” sneered the Jew, catching the boy by the arm. “We’ll cure you of that, my young master.”    the Jew inflicted a smart blow on Oliver shoulders with the club; and was raising it for a second, when the girl, rushing forward, wrested it from Oliver hand. the girl flung it into the fire, with a force that brought some of the glowing coals whirling out into the room.    “I won’t stand by and see it done, Fagin,” cried the girl. “You’ve got the boy, and what more would you have? Let the boy be let the boy be or I shall put that mark on some of you, that will bring me to the gallows before my time.”    the girl stamped the girl foot violently on the floor as the girl vented this threat; and with the girl lips compressed, and the girl hands clenched, looked alternately at the Jew and the other robber: the girl face quite colourless from the passion of rage into which the girl had gradually worked the girl.    “Why, Nancy!” said the Jew, in a soothing tone; after a pause, during which the Jew and Sikes had stared at one another in a disconcerted manner; “you, you’re more clever than ever to night. Ha! ha! my dear, you are acting beautifully.”    “Am I!” said the girl. “Take care I don’t overdo it. You will be the worse for it, Fagin, if I do; and so I tell you in good time to keep clear of me.”    There is something about a roused woman: especially if a roused woman add to all a roused woman other strong passions, the fierce impulses of recklessness and despair; which few men like to provoke. the Jew saw that it would be hopeless to affect any further mistake regarding the reality of Miss Nancy rage; and, shrinking involuntarily back a few paces, cast a glance, half imploring and half cowardly, at Sikes: as if to hint that the Jew was the fittest person to pursue the dialogue.    Sikes, thus mutely appealed to; and possibly feeling Sikes personal pride and influence interested in the immediate reduction of Nancy Nancy to reason; gave utterance to about a couple of score of curses and threats, the rapid production of which reflected great credit on the fertility of Sikes invention. As they produced no visible effect on the object against whom they were discharged, however, Sikes resorted to more tangible arguments.    “What do you mean by this?” said Sikes; backing the inquiry with a very common imprecation concerning the most beautiful of human features: which, if this were heard above, only once out of every fifty thousand times that it is uttered below, would render blindness as common a disorder as measles: “what do you mean by it? Burn my body! Do you know who you are, and what you are?”    “Oh, yes, I know all about it,” replied the girl, laughing hysterically; and shaking the girl head from side to side, with a poor assumption of indifference.    “Well, then, keep quiet,” rejoined Sikes, with a growl like that Sikes was accustomed to use when addressing Sikes dog, “or I’ll quiet you for a good long time to come.”    the girl laughed again: even less composedly than before; and, darting a hasty look at Sikes, turned the girl face aside, and bit the girl lip till the blood came.    “You’re a nice one,” added Sikes, as Sikes surveyed Sikes with a contemptuous air, “to take up the humane and gen teel side! A pretty subject for the child, as you call the child, to make a friend of!”    “God Almighty help me, I am!” cried the girl passionately; “and I wish I had been struck dead in the street, or had changed places with them we passed so near to night, before I had lent a hand in bringing him here. him a thief, a liar, a devil, all that bad, from this night forth. Isn’t that enough for the old wretch, without blows?”    “Come, come, Sikes,” said the Jew appealing to the Jew in a remonstratory tone, and motioning towards the boys, who were eagerly attentive to all that passed; “we must have civil words; civil words, Bill.”    “Civil words!” cried the girl, whose passion was frightful to see. “Civil words, you villain! Yes, you deserve ’em from the Jew. I thieved for you when I was a child not half as old as this!” pointing to Oliver. “I have been in the same trade, and in the same service, for twelve years since. Don’t you know it? Speak out! Don’t you know it?”    “Well, well,” replied the Jew, with an attempt at pacification; “and, if you have, it your living!”    “Aye, it is!” returned the girl; not speaking, but pouring out the words in one continuous and vehement scream. “It is my living; and the cold, wet, dirty streets are my home; and you’re the wretch that drove me to dirty streets long ago, and that’ll keep me there, day and night, day and night, till I die!”    “I shall do you a mischief!” interposed the Jew, goaded by these reproaches; “a mischief worse than that, if you say much more!”    the girl said nothing more; but, tearing the girl hair and dress in a transport of passion, made such a rush at the Jew as would probably have left signal marks of the girl revenge upon him, had not the girl wrists been seized by Sikes at the right moment; upon which, the girl made a few ineffectual struggles, and fainted.    “the girl all right now,” said Sikes, laying the girl down in a corner. “the girl uncommon strong in the arms, when the girl up in this way.”    the Jew wiped the Jew forehead: and smiled, as if the Jew were a relief to have the disturbance over; but neither the Jew, nor Sikes, nor the dog, nor the boys, seemed to consider it in any other light than a common occurance incidental to business.    “It the worst of having to do with women,” said the Jew, replacing the Jew club; “but they’re clever, and we can’t get on, in we line, without ’em. Charley, show Oliver to bed.”    “I suppose Oliver’d better not wear Oliver best clothes tomorrow, Fagin, had Oliver?” inquired Charley Bates.    “Certainly not,” replied the Jew, reciprocating the grin with which Charley put the question.    Charley Bates, apparently much delighted with Charley Bates commission, took the cleft stick: and led Oliver into an adjacent kitchen, where there were two or three of the beds on which Oliver had slept before; and here, with many uncontrollable bursts of laughter, Oliver produced the identical old suit of clothes which Oliver had so much congratulated Oliver upon leaving off at Mr. Brownlow; and the accidental display of which, to Fagin, by the Jew who purchased them, had been the very first clue received, of Oliver whereabout.    “Put off the smart ones,” said Charley, “and I’ll give ’em to Fagin to take care of. What fun it is!”    Oliver unwillingly complied. Charley Bates rolling up his best clothes under Charley Bates arm, departed from the room, leaving Oliver in the dark, and locking the door behind Oliver.    The noise of Charley laughter, and the voice of Miss Betsy, who opportunely arrived to throw water over her friend, and perform other feminine offices for the promotion of her recovery, might have kept many people awake under more happy circumstances than those in which Oliver was placed. But Oliver was sick and weary; and Oliver soon fell sound asleep.  CHAPTER 18
XVII.  OLIVER’S DESTINY CONTINUING UNPROPITIOUS, BRINGS A GREAT MAN TO LONDON TO INJURE A GREAT MAN REPUTATION    It is the custom on the stage, in all good murderous melodramas, to present the tragic and the comic scenes, in as regular alternation, as the layers of red and white in a side of streaky bacon. The hero sinks upon his straw bed, weighed down by fetters and misfortunes; in the next scene, his faithful but unconscious squire regales the audience with a comic song. We behold, with throbbing bosoms, the heroine in the grasp of a proud and ruthless baron: her virtue and her life alike in danger, drawing forth her dagger to preserve the one at the cost of the other; and just as We expectations are wrought up to the highest pitch, a whistle is heard, and We are straightway transported to the great hall of the castle; where a grey headed seneschal sings a funny chorus with a funnier body of vassals, who are free of all sorts of places, from church vaults to palaces, and roam about in company, carolling perpetually.    Such changes appear absurd; but Such changes are not so unnatural as Such changes would seem at first sight. The transitions in real life from well spread boards to death beds, and from mourning weeds to holiday garments, are not a whit less startling; only, there, we are busy actors, instead of passive lookers on, which makes a vast difference. The actors in the mimic life of the theatre, are blind to violent transitions and abrupt impulses of passion or feeling, which, presented before the eyes of mere spectators, are at once condemned as outrageous and preposterous.    As sudden shiftings of the scene, and rapid changes of time and place, are not only sanctioned in books by long usage, but are by many considered as the great art of authorship: an author skill in his craft being, by such critics, chiefly estimated with relation to the dilemmas in which his leaves his characters at the end of every chapter: this brief introduction to the present one may perhaps be deemed unnecessary. If so, let it be considered a delicate intimation on the part of the historian that the historian is going back to the town in which Oliver Twist was born; the reader taking it for granted that there are good and substantial reasons for making the journey, or the historian would not be invited to proceed upon such an expedition.    Mr. Bumble emerged at early morning from the workhouse gate, and walked with portly carriage and commanding steps, up the High Street. Mr. Bumble was in the full bloom and pride of beadlehood; Mr. Bumble cocked hat and coat were dazzling in the morning sun; Mr. Bumble clutched Mr. Bumble cane with the vigorous tenacity of health and power. Mr. Bumble always carried Mr. Bumble head high; but this morning it was higher than usual. There was an abstraction in Mr. Bumble eye, an elevation in Mr. Bumble air, which might have warned an observant stranger that thoughts were passing in the beadle mind, too great for utterance.    Mr. Bumble stopped not to converse with the small shopkeepers and others who spoke to Mr. Bumble, deferentially, as Mr. Bumble passed along. Mr. Bumble merely returned their salutations with a wave of Mr. Bumble hand, and relaxed not in Mr. Bumble dignified pace, until Mr. Bumble reached the farm where Mrs. Mann tended the infant paupers with parochial care.    “Drat that beadle!” said Mrs. Mann, hearing the well known shaking at the garden gate. “If it isn’t him at this time in the morning! Lauk, Mr. Bumble, only think of its being you! Well, dear me, it is a pleasure, this is! Come into the parlour, sir, please.”    The first sentence was addressed to Susan; and the exclamations of delight were uttered to Mr. Bumble: as the good lady unlocked the garden gate: and showed Mr. Bumble, with great attention and respect, into the house.    “Mrs. Mann,” said Mr. Bumble; not sitting upon, or dropping himself into a seat, as any common jackanapes would: but letting himself gradually and slowly down into a chair; “Mrs. Mann, ma’am, good morning.”    “Well, and good morning to you, sir,” replied Mrs. Mann, with many smiles; “and hoping you find yourself well, sir!”    “So so, Mrs. Mann,” replied the beadle. “A porochial life is not a bed of roses, Mrs. Mann.”    “Ah, that it isn’t indeed, Mr. Bumble,” rejoined the good lady. And all the infant paupers might have chorussed the rejoinder with great propriety, if all the infant paupers had heard it.    “A porochial life, ma’am,” continued Mr. Bumble, striking the table with Mr. Bumble cane, “is a life of worrit, and vexation, and hardihood; but all public characters, as I may say, must suffer prosecution.”    Mrs. Mann, not very well knowing what the beadle meant, raised Mrs. Mann hands with a look of sympathy, and sighed.    “Ah! You may well sigh, Mrs. Mann!” said the beadle.    Finding Mrs. Mann had done right, Mrs. Mann sighed again: evidently to the satisfaction of the public character: who, repressing a complacent smile by looking sternly at Mrs. Mann cocked hat, said,    “Mrs. Mann, I am going to London.”    “Lauk, Mr. Bumble!” cried Mrs. Mann, starting back.    “To London, ma’am,” resumed the inflexible beadle, “by coach. I and two paupers, Mrs. Mann! A legal action is a coming on, about a settlement; and the board has appointed me me, Mrs. Mann to dispose to the matter before the Clerkinwell Sessions.    And I very much question,” added Mr. Bumble, drawing Mr. Bumble up, “whether the Clerkinwell Sessions will not find the Clerkinwell Sessions in the wrong box before the Clerkinwell Sessions have done with Mr. Bumble.”    “Oh! you mustn’t be too hard upon the Clerkinwell Sessions, Mr. Bumble,” said Mrs. Mann, coaxingly.    “the Clerkinwell Sessions have brought it upon the Clerkinwell Sessions, ma’am,” replied Mr. Bumble; “and if the Clerkinwell Sessions find that the Clerkinwell Sessions come off rather worse than the Clerkinwell Sessions expected, the Clerkinwell Sessions have only the Clerkinwell Sessions to thank.”    There was so much determination and depth of purpose about the menacing manner in which Mr. Bumble delivered Mr. Bumble of these words, that Mrs. Mann appeared quite awed by them. At length Mrs. Mann said,    “You’re going by coach, Mr. Bumble? I thought it was always usual to send You’re going by coach, sir paupers in carts.”    “That when You’re going by coach, sir’re ill, Mrs. Mann,” said the beadle. “We put paupers in carts into open carts in the rainy weather, to prevent paupers in carts taking cold.”    “Oh!” said Mrs. Mann.    “The opposition coach contracts for these two; and takes these two cheap,” said Mr. Bumble. “these two are both in a very low state, and we find it would come two pound cheaper to move ’em than to bury ’em that is, if we can throw ’em upon another parish, which I think we shall be able to do, if they don’t die upon the road to spite we. Ha! ha! ha!”    When Mr. Bumble had laughed a little while, Mr. Bumble eyes again encountered the cocked hat; and Mr. Bumble became grave.    “we are forgetting business, ma’am,” said the beadle; “here is your porochial stipend for the month.”    Mr. Bumble produced some silver money rolled up in paper, from Mr. Bumble pocket book; and requested a receipt: which Mrs. Mann wrote.    “It very much blotted, sir,” said the farmer of infants; “but it formal enough, I dare say. Thank you, Mr. Bumble, sir, I am very much obliged to sir, I’m sure.”    Mr. Bumble nodded, blandly, in acknowledgment of Mrs. Mann curtsey; and inquired how the children were.    “Bless their dear little hearts!” said Mrs. Mann with emotion, “their’re as well as can be, the dears! Of course, except the two that died last week. And little Dick.”    “Isn’t that boy no better?” inquired Mr. Bumble.    Mrs. Mann shook Mrs. Mann head.    “that boy a ill conditioned, wicious, bad disposed porochial child that,” said Mr. Bumble angrily. “Where is that boy?”    “I’ll bring that boy to sir in one minute, sir,” replied Mrs. Mann. “Here, you Dick!”    After some calling, Dick was discovered. Having had Dick face put under the pump, and dried upon Mrs. Mann gown, Dick was led into the awful presence of Mr. Bumble, the beadle.    The child was pale and thin; The child cheeks were sunken; and The child eyes large and bright. The scanty parish dress, the livery of The child misery, hung loosely on The child feeble body; and The child young limbs had wasted away, like those of an old man.    Such was the little being who stood trembling beneath Mr. Bumble glance; not daring to lift the little being who stood trembling beneath Mr. Bumble glance from the floor; and dreading even to hear the beadle voice.    “Can’t you look at the gentleman, you obstinate boy?” said Mrs. Mann.    The child meekly raised his eyes, and encountered those of Mr. Bumble.    “What the matter with you, DickDick?” inquired Mr. Bumble, with well timed jocularity.    “Nothing, sir,” replied The child faintly.    “I should think not,” said Mrs. Mann, who had of course laughed very much at Mr. Bumble.    “You want for nothing, I’m sure.”    “I should like ” faltered The child.    “Hey day!” interposed Mrs. Mann, “I suppose you’re going to say that you do want for something, now? Why, you little wretch ”    “Stop, Mrs. Mann, stop!” said beadle, raising beadle hand with a show of authority. “Like what, sir, eh?”    “I should like,” faltered The child, “if somebody that can write, would put a few words down for me on a piece of paper, and fold a piece of paper up and seal a piece of paper, and keep a piece of paper for me, after I am laid in the ground.”    “Why, what does the boy mean?” exclaimed Mr. Bumble, on whom the earnest manner and wan aspect of The child had made some impression: accustomed as the boy was to such things. “What do you mean, sir?”    “I should like,” said the child, “to leave my dear love to poor Oliver Twist; and to let sir know how often I have sat by me and cried to think of sir wandering about in the dark nights with nobody to help sir. And I should like to tell sir,” said The child pressing sir small hands together, and speaking with great fervour, “that I was glad to die when I was very young; for, perhaps, if I had lived to be a man, and had grown old, my little sister who is in Heaven, might forget me, or be unlike me; and it would be so much happier if we were both children there together.”    Mr. Bumble surveyed the little speaker, from head to foot, with indescribable astonishment; and, turning to Mr. Bumble companion, said, “They’re all in one story, Mrs. Mann. That out dacious Oliver had demogalized all in one story, Mrs. Mann!”    “I couldn’t have believed it, sir” said Mrs Mann, holding up Mrs Mann , and looking malignantly at Dick. “I never see such a hardened little wretch!”    “Take Dick away, ma’am!” said Mr. Bumble imperiously. “This must be stated to the board, Mrs. Mann.”    “I hope the gentleman will understand that the gentleman isn’t my fault, sir?” said Mrs. Mann, whimpering pathetically.    “They shall understand that, ma’am; They shall be acquainted with the true state of the case,” said Mr. Bumble. “There; take Mr. Bumble away, I can’t bear the sight on Mr. Bumble.”    Dick was immediately taken away, and locked up in the coal cellar. Mr. Bumble shortly afterwards took Mr. Bumble off, to prepare for Mr. Bumble journey.    At six o’clock next morning, Mr. Bumble: having exchanged Mr. Bumble cocked hat for a round one, and encased Mr. Bumble person in a blue great coat with a cape to it: took Mr. Bumble place on the outside of the coach, accompanied by the criminals whose settlement was disputed; with whom, in due course of time, Mr. Bumble arrived in London.    Mr. Bumble experienced no other crosses on the way, than those which originated in the perverse behaviour of the two paupers, who persisted in shivering, and complaining of the cold, in a manner which, Mr. Bumble declared, caused Mr. Bumble teeth to chatter in Mr. Bumble head, and made Mr. Bumble feel quite uncomfortable; although Mr. Bumble had a great coat on.    Having disposed of these evil minded persons for the night, Mr. Bumble sat Mr. Bumble down in the house at which the coach stopped; and took a temperate dinner of steaks, oyster sauce, and porter. Putting a glass of hot gin and water on the chimney piece, Mr. Bumble drew Mr. Bumble chair to the fire; and, with sundry moral reflections on the too prevalent sin of discontent and complaining, composed Mr. Bumble to read the paper.    The very first paragraph upon which Mr. Bumble eye rested, was the following advertisement.    “FIVE GUINEAS REWARD    “Whereas a young boy, named Oliver Twist, absconded, or was enticed, on Thursday evening last, from his home, at Pentonville; and has not since been heard of. The above reward will be paid to any person who will give such information as will lead to the discovery of the said Oliver Twist, or tend to throw any light upon Oliver Twist previous history, in which the advertiser is, for many reasons, warmly interested.”    And then followed a full description of Oliver dress, person, appearance, and disappearance: with the name and address of Mr. Brownlow at full length.    Mr. Bumble opened Mr. Bumble eyes; read the advertisement, slowly and carefully, three several times; and in something more than five minutes was on Mr. Bumble way to Pentonville: having actually, in Mr. Bumble excitement, left the glass of hot gin and water, untasted.    “Is Mr. Brownlow at home?” inquired Mr. Bumble of the girl who opened the door.    To this inquiry the girl who opened the door returned the not uncommon, but rather evasive reply of “I don’t know; where do you come from?”    Mr. Bumble no sooner uttered Oliver name, in explanation of Oliver errand, than Mrs. Bedwin, who had been listening at the parlour door, hastened into the passage in a breathless state.    “Come in, come in,” said the old lady: “I knew we should hear of Oliver. Poor dear! I knew we should! I was certain of it. Bless Oliver heart! I said so all along.”    Having heard this, the old lady hurried back into the parlour again; and seating the old lady on a sofa, burst into tears. The girl, who was not quite so susceptible, had run upstairs meanwhile; and now returned with a request that Mr. Bumble would follow the old lady immediately: which Mr. Bumble did.    Mr. Bumble was shown into the little back study, where sat Mr. Brownlow and Mr. Bumble friend Mr. Grimwig, with decanters and glasses before the little back study, where sat Mr. Brownlow and his friend Mr. Grimwig. The latter gentleman at once burst into the exclamation:    “A beadle. A parish beadle, or I’ll eat his head.”    “Pray don’t interrupt just now,” said Mr. Brownlow. “Take a seat, will you?”    Mr. Bumble sat Mr. Bumble down; quite confounded by the oddity of Mr. Grimwig manner. Mr. Brownlow moved the lamp, so as to obtain an uninterrupted view of the beadle countenance; and said, with a little impatience,    “Now, sir, you come in consequence of having seen the advertisement?”    “Yes, sir,” said Mr. Bumble.    “And you ARE a beadle, are you not?” inquired Mr. Grimwig.    “I am a porochial beadle, gentlemen,” rejoined Mr. Bumble proudly.    “Of course,” observed Mr. Grimwig aside to Mr. Grimwig friend, “I knew Mr. Grimwig was. A beadle all over!”    Mr. Brownlow gently shook Mr. Brownlow to impose silence on his friend, and resumed:    “Do you know where Mr. Grimwig is now?”    “No more than nobody,” replied Mr. Bumble.    “Well, what do you know of Mr. Grimwig?” inquired The latter gentleman. “Speak out, his friend, if you have anything to say. Mr. Bumble put down Mr. Bumble hat; unbuttoned Mr. Bumble coat; folded Mr. Bumble arms; inclined his head in a retrospective manner; and, after a few moments’ reflection, commenced Mr. Bumble story.    It would be tedious if given in the beadle words: occupying, as it did, some twenty minutes in the telling; but the sum and substance of it was, that Oliver was a foundling, born of low and vicious parents. That Oliver had, from Oliver birth, displayed no better qualities than treachery, ingratitude, and malice. That Oliver had terminated Oliver brief career in the place of his birth, by making a sanguinary and cowardly attack on an unoffending lad, and running away in the night time from Oliver master house. It was too late to do it now, however; so Mr. Bumble shook his head gravely, and, pocketing the five guineas, withdrew.    Mr. Brownlow paced the room to and fro for some minutes; evidently so much disturbed by the beadle tale, that even Mr. Grimwig forbore to vex Mr. Bumble further.    At length Mr. Bumble stopped, and rang the bell violently.    “Mrs. Bedwin,” said Mr. Brownlow, when the housekeeper appeared; “that boy, Oliver, is an imposter.”    “It can’t be, sir. Why didn’t you take my advise in the beginning; you would if sir hadn’t had a fever, I suppose, eh? sir was interesting, wasn’t sir? Interesting! Bah!” And Mr. Grimwig poked the fire with a flourish.    “sir was a dear, grateful, gentle child, sir,” retorted Mrs. Bedwin, indignantly. “I know what children are, sir; and have done these forty years; and people who can’t say the same, shouldn’t say anything about children. That my opinion!”    This was a hard hit at Mr. Grimwig, who was a bachelor. Oliver heart sank within Oliver, when Mr. Brownlow thought of Mr. Brownlow good friends; it was well for Mr. Brownlow that Mr. Brownlow could not know what they had heard, or it might have broken outright. XVIII.  HOW OLIVER PASSED HIS TIME IN THE IMPROVING SOCIETY OF HIS REPUTABLE FRIENDS    About noon next day, when the Dodger and Master Bates had gone out to pursue the Dodger and Master Bates customary avocations, Mr. Fagin took the opportunity of reading Oliver a long lecture on the crying sin of ingratitude; of which Mr. Fagin clearly demonstrated Mr. Fagin had been guilty, to no ordinary extent, in wilfully absenting Mr. Fagin from the society of Mr. Fagin anxious friends; and, still more, in endeavouring to escape from his anxious friends after so much trouble and expense had been incurred in Mr. Fagin recovery. Mr. Fagin laid great stress on the fact of Mr. Fagin having taken Oliver in, and cherished Mr. Fagin, when, without Mr. Fagin timely aid, Mr. Fagin might have perished with hunger; and Mr. Fagin related the dismal and affecting history of a young lad whom, in Mr. Fagin philanthropy, Mr. Fagin had succoured under parallel circumstances, but who, proving unworthy of Mr. Fagin confidence and evincing a desire to communicate with the police, had unfortunately come to be hanged at the Old Bailey one morning. Mr. Fagin did not seek to conceal Mr. Fagin share in the catastrophe, but lamented with tears in Mr. Fagin eyes that the wrong headed and treacherous behaviour of the young person in question, had rendered it necessary that Mr. Fagin should become the victim of certain evidence for the crown: which, if it were not precisely true, was indispensably necessary for the safety of Mr. Fagin (Mr. Fagin) and a few select friends. Mr. Fagin concluded by drawing a rather disagreeable picture of the discomforts of hanging; and, with great friendliness and politeness of manner, expressed Mr. Fagin anxious hopes that Mr. Fagin might never be obliged to submit Oliver Twist to that unpleasant operation.    Little Oliver blood ran cold, as Little Oliver listened to the Jew words, and imperfectly comprehended the dark threats conveyed in the Jew words. That it was possible even for justice itself to confound the innocent with the guilty when they were in accidental companionship, Little Oliver knew already; and that deeply laid plans for the destruction of inconveniently knowing or over communicative persons, had been really devised and carried out by the Jew on more occasions than one, Little Oliver thought by no means unlikely, when Little Oliver recollected the general nature of the altercations between that gentleman and Mr. Sikes: which seemed to bear reference to some foregone conspiracy of the kind. As Little Oliver glanced timidly up, and met the Jew searching look, Little Oliver felt that Little Oliver pale face and trembling limbs were neither unnoticed nor unrelished by that wary old gentleman.    the Jew, smiling hideously, patted Little Oliver on the head, and said, that if Little Oliver kept Little Oliver quiet, and applied Little Oliver to business, Little Oliver saw they would be very good friends yet. Then, taking Little Oliver hat, and covering Little Oliver with an old patched great coat, Little Oliver went out, and locked the room door behind Little Oliver.    And so Little Oliver remained all that day, and for the greater part of many subsequent days, seeing nobody, between early morning and midnight, and left during the long hours to commune with Little Oliver own thoughts. Which, never failing to revert to Little Oliver kind friends, and the opinion his kind friends must long ago have formed of Little Oliver, were sad indeed.    After the lapse of a week or so, the Jew left the room door unlocked; and the Jew was at liberty to wander about the house.    It was a very dirty place. The rooms upstairs had great high wooden chimney pieces and large doors, with panelled walls and cornices to the ceiling; which, although The rooms upstairs were black with neglect and dust, were ornamented in various ways. From all of these tokens Little Oliver concluded that a long time ago, before the Jew was born, it had belonged to better people, and had perhaps been quite gay and handsome: dismal and dreary as it looked now.    Spiders had built Spiders webs in the angles of the walls and ceilings; and sometimes, when Little Oliver walked softly into a room, the mice would scamper across the floor, and run back terrified to the mice holes. With their holes, there was neither sight nor sound of any living thing; and often, when living grew dark, and Little Oliver was tired of wandering from room to room, Little Oliver would crouch in the corner of the passage by the street door, to be as near living people as Little Oliver could; and would remain there, listening and counting the hours, until the Jew or the boys returned.    In all the rooms, the mouldering shutters were fast closed: the bars which held the mouldering shutters were screwed tight into the wood; the only light which was admitted, stealing the wood way through round holes at the top: which made the rooms more gloomy, and filled the mouldering shutters with strange shadows. There was a back garret window with rusty bars outside, which had no shutter; and out of this, Little Oliver often gazed with a melancholy face for hours together; but nothing was to be descried from it but a confused and crowded mass of housetops, blackened chimneys, and gable ends. Sometimes, indeed, a grizzly head might be seen, peering over the parapet wall of a distant house; but a grizzly head was quickly withdrawn again; and as the window of Little Oliver observatory was nailed down, and dimmed with the rain and smoke of years, it was as much as Little Oliver could do to make out the forms of the different objects beyond, without making any attempt to be seen or heard, which Little Oliver had as much chance of being, as if Little Oliver had lived inside the ball of St. Paul Cathedral.    One afternoon, the Dodger and Master Bates being engaged out that evening, the first named young gentleman took it into the first named young gentleman to evince some anxiety regarding the decoration of the first named young gentleman person (to do the first named young gentleman justice, this was by no means an habitual weakness with the first named young gentleman); and, with this end and aim, the first named young gentleman condescendingly commanded Little Oliver to assist Little Oliver in the first named young gentleman toilet, straightway.    Little Oliver was but too glad to make Little Oliver useful; too happy to have some faces, however bad, to look upon; too desirous to conciliate those about Little Oliver when Little Oliver could honestly do so; to throw any objection in the way of this proposal. XIX.  IN WHICH A NOTABLE PLAN IS DISCUSSED AND DETERMINED ON    A NOTABLE PLAN was a chill, damp, windy night, when the Jew: buttoning his great coat tight round his shrivelled body, and pulling the collar up over his ears so as completely to obscure the lower part of his face: emerged from his den. his paused on the step as the door was locked and chained behind his; and having listened while the boys made all secure, and until the boys retreating footsteps were no longer audible, slunk down the street as quickly as his could. The mud lay thick upon the stones, and a black mist hung over the streets; the rain fell sluggishly down, and everything felt cold and clammy to the touch. It seemed just the night when It befitted such a being as the Jew: buttoning his great coat tight to be abroad. As his glided stealthily along, creeping beneath the shelter of the walls and doorways, his seemed like some loathsome reptile, engendered in the slime and darkness through which his moved: crawling forth, by night, in search of some rich offal for a meal.    his kept on his course, through many winding and narrow ways, until his reached Bethnal Green; then, turning suddenly off to the left, his soon became involved in a maze of the mean and dirty streets which abound in that close and densely populated quarter.    the Jew: buttoning his great coat tight was evidently too familiar with the ground his traversed to be at all bewildered, either by the darkness of the night, or the intricacies of the way. his hurried through several alleys and streets, and at length turned into one, lighted only by a single lamp at the farther end. XX.  WHEREIN OLIVER IS DELIVERED OVER TO MR. WILLIAM SIKES    When Oliver awoke in the morning, Oliver was a good deal surprised to find that a new pair of shoes, with strong thick soles, had been placed at Oliver bedside; and that Oliver old shoes had been removed. It was a history of the lives and trials of great criminals; and the pages were soiled and thumbed with use. Here, the Jew read of dreadful crimes that made the blood run cold; of secret murders that had been committed by the lonely wayside; of bodies hidden from the eye of man in deep pits and wells: which would not keep man in deep pits and wells down, deep as man in deep pits and wells were, but had yielded man in deep pits and wells up at last, after many years, and so maddened the murderers with the sight, that in man in deep pits and wells horror man in deep pits and wells had confessed man in deep pits and wells guilt, and yelled for the gibbet to end man in deep pits and wells agony. Indeed, the worthy gentleman, stimulated perhaps by the immediate prospect of being on active service, was in great spirits and good humour; in proof whereof, it may be here remarked, that he humourously drank all the beer at a draught, and did not utter, on a rough calculation, more than four score oaths during the whole progress of the meal. XXI.  THE EXPEDITION    It was a cheerless morning when they got into the street; blowing and raining hard; and the clouds looking dull and stormy. The night had been very wet: large pools of water had collected in the road: and the kennels were overflowing. There was a faint glimmering of the coming day in the sky; but a faint glimmering of the coming day in the sky rather aggravated than relieved the gloom of the scene: the sombre light only serving to pale that which the street lamps afforded, without shedding any warmer or brighter tints upon the wet house tops, and dreary streets. There appeared to be nobody stirring in that quarter of the town; the windows of the houses were all closely shut; and the streets through which they passed, were noiseless and empty.    By the time they had turned into the Bethnal Green Road, the day had fairly begun to break. Many of the street lamps were already extinguished; a few country waggons were slowly toiling on, towards London; now and then, a stage coach, covered with mud, rattled briskly by: the driver bestowing, as he passed, an admonitory lash upon the heavy waggoner who, by keeping on the wrong side of the road, had endangered he arriving at the office, a quarter of a minute after he time. The public houses, with gas lights burning inside, were already open. By degrees, other shops began to be unclosed, and a few scattered people were met with. Then, came straggling groups of labourers going to labourers work; then, men and women with fish baskets on women heads; donkey carts laden with vegetables; chaise carts filled with live stock or whole carcasses of meat; milk women with pails; an unbroken concourse of people, trudging out with various supplies to the eastern suburbs of the town. As they approached the City, the noise and traffic gradually increased; when they threaded the streets between Shoreditch and Smithfield, it had swelled into a roar of sound and bustle. it was as light as it was likely to be, till night came on again, and the busy morning of half the London population had begun.    Turning down Sun Street and Crown Street, and crossing Finsbury square, Mr. Sikes struck, by way of Chiswell Street, into Barbican: thence into Long Lane, and so into Smithfield; from which latter place arose a tumult of discordant sounds that filled Oliver Twist with amazement.    It was market morning. The ground was covered, nearly ankle deep, with filth and mire; a thick steam, perpetually rising from the reeking bodies of the cattle, and mingling with the fog, which seemed to rest upon the chimney tops, hung heavily above. All the pens in the centre of the large area, and as many temporary pens as could be crowded into the vacant space, were filled with sheep; tied up to posts by the gutter side were long lines of beasts and oxen, three or four deep. Countrymen, butchers, drovers, hawkers, boys, thieves, idlers, and vagabonds of every low grade, were mingled together in a mass; the whistling of drovers, the barking dogs, the bellowing and plunging of the oxen, the bleating of sheep, the grunting and squeaking of pigs, the cries of hawkers, the shouts, oaths, and quarrelling on all sides; the ringing of bells and roar of voices, that issued from every public house; the crowding, pushing, driving, beating, whooping and yelling; the hideous and discordant dim that resounded from every corner of the market; and the unwashed, unshaven, squalid, and dirty figures constantly running to and fro, and bursting in and out of the throng; rendered it a stunning and bewildering scene, which quite confounded the senses.    Mr. Sikes, dragging Oliver after Oliver, elbowed Oliver way through the thickest of the crowd, and bestowed very little attention on the numerous sights and sounds, which so astonished the boy. the boy nodded, twice or thrice, to a passing friend; and, resisting as many invitations to take a morning dram, pressed steadily onward, until Mr. Sikes, dragging Oliver after him were clear of the turmoil, and had made Mr. Sikes, dragging Oliver after him way through Hosier Lane into Holborn.    “Now, young ’un!” said Sikes, looking up at the clock of St. Andrew Church, “hard upon seven! you must step out. XXIII.  WHICH CONTAINS THE SUBSTANCE OF A PLEASANT CONVERSATION BETWEEN MR. BUMBLE AND A LADY; AND SHOWS THAT EVEN A BEADLE MAY BE SUSCEPTIBLE ON SOME POINTS    The night was bitter cold. The snow lay on the ground, frozen into a hard thick crust, so that only the heaps that had drifted into byways and corners were affected by the sharp wind that howled abroad: which, as if expending increased fury on such prey as it found, caught it savagely up in clouds, and, whirling it into a thousand misty eddies, scattered it in air. Bleak, dark, and piercing cold, it was a night for the well housed and fed to draw round the bright fire and thank God they were at home; and for the homeless, starving wretch to lay him down and die. Many hunger worn outcasts close Many hunger eyes in our bare streets, at such times, who, let Many hunger crimes have been what Many hunger may, can hardly open their crimes in a more bitter world.    Such was the aspect of out of doors affairs, when Mrs. Corney, sat herself down before a cheerful fire in herself own little room, and glanced, with no small degree of complacency, at a small round table: on which stood a tray of corresponding size, furnished with all necessary materials for the most grateful meal that matrons enjoy. In fact, Mrs. Corney was about to solace Mrs. Corney with a cup of tea. As Mrs. Corney glanced from the table to the fireplace, where the smallest of all possible kettles was singing a small song in a small voice, Mrs. Corney inward satisfaction evidently increased, so much so, indeed, that Mrs. Corney smiled.    “Well!” said the matron, leaning the matron elbow on the table, and looking reflectively at a cheerful fire in her own little room; “I’m sure we have all on we a great deal to be grateful for! A great deal, if we did but know it. Ah!”    Mrs. Corney shook Mrs. Corney head mournfully, as if deploring the mental blindness of those paupers who did not know it; and thrusting a silver spoon (private property) into the inmost recesses of a two ounce tin tea caddy, proceeded to make the tea.    How slight a thing will disturb the equanimity of our frail minds! The black teapot, being very small and easily filled, ran over while Mrs. Corney was moralising; and the water slightly scalded Mrs. Corney hand.    “Drat the pot!” said the matron, setting the matron down very hastily on the hob; “a little stupid thing, that only holds a couple of cups! What use is it of, to anybody! Except,” said Mrs. Corney, pausing, “except to a poor desolate creature like me. Oh dear!”    With these words, the matron dropped into the matron chair, and, once more resting the matron elbow on the table, thought of the matron solitary fate. The small teapot, and the single cup, had awakened in the matron mind sad recollections of Mr. Corney (who had not been dead more than five and twenty years); and the matron was overpowered.    “I shall never get another!” said Mrs. Corney, pettishly; “I shall never get another like another.”    Whether this remark bore reference to the husband, or the teapot, is uncertain. It might have been the latter; for Mrs. Corney looked at it as Mrs. Corney spoke; and took it up afterwards. Some of the old women always die when I’m at meals. Don’t stand there, letting the cold air in, don’t. What amiss now, eh?”    “Nothing, ma’am, nothing,” replied a man voice.    “Dear me!” exclaimed the matron, in a much sweeter tone, “is that Mr. Bumble?”    “At your service, ma’am,” said Mr. Bumble, who had been stopping outside to rub his shoes clean, and to shake the snow off his coat; and who now made his appearance, bearing the cocked hat in one hand and a bundle in the other. “Shall I shut the door, ma’am?”    Mrs. Corney modestly hesitated to reply, lest there should be any impropriety in holding an interview with Mr. Bumble, with closed doors. XXIV.  TREATS ON A VERY POOR SUBJECT. BUT IS A SHORT ONE, AND MAY BE FOUND OF IMPORTANCE IN THIS HISTORY    It was no unfit messenger of death, who had disturbed the quiet of the matron room. Her body was bent by age; Her limbs trembled with palsy; Her face, distorted into a mumbling leer, resembled more the grotesque shaping of some wild pencil, than the work of Nature hand.    Alas! How few of Nature faces are left alone to gladden us with How few of Nature faces beauty! The cares, and sorrows, and hungerings, of the world, change The cares, and sorrows, and hungerings, of the world as The cares, and sorrows, and hungerings, of the world change hearts; and it is only when those passions sleep, and have lost those passions hold for ever, that the troubled clouds pass off, and leave Heaven surface clear. It is a common thing for the countenances of the dead, even in that fixed and rigid state, to subside into the long forgotten expression of sleeping infancy, and settle into the very look of early life; so calm, so peaceful, do they grow again, that those who knew they in they happy childhood, kneel by the coffin side in awe, and see the Angel even upon earth.    The old crone tottered along the passages, and up the stairs, muttering some indistinct answers to the chidings of her companion; being at length compelled to pause for breath, her gave the light into her hand, and remained behind to follow as her might: while the more nimble superior made her way to the room where the sick woman lay.    It was a bare garret room, with a dim light burning at the farther end. There was another old woman watching by the bed; the parish apothecary apprentice was standing by the fire, making a toothpick out of a quill.    “Cold night, Mrs. Corney,” said this young gentleman, as the matron entered.    “Very cold, indeed, sir,” replied the mistress, in the mistress most civil tones, and dropping a curtsey as the mistress spoke.    “You should get better coals out of your contractors,” said the apothecary deputy, breaking a lump on the top of the fire with the rusty poker; “these are not at all the sort of thing for a cold night.”    “these’re the board choosing, sir,” returned the matron. “The least these could do, would be to keep us pretty warm: for us places are hard enough.”    The conversation was here interrupted by a moan from the sick woman.    “Oh!” said the young man, turning the young man face towards the bed, as if the young man had previously quite forgotten the patient, “it all U.P. there, Mrs. Corney.”    “It is, is it, sir?” asked the matron.    “If the matron lasts a couple of hours, I shall be surprised,” said the apothecary apprentice, intent upon the toothpick point. “It a break up of the system altogether. Is the matron dozing, old lady?”    The attendant stooped over the bed, to ascertain; and nodded in the affirmative.    “Then perhaps the matron’ll go off in that way, if you don’t make a row,” said the young man. “Put the light on the floor. the matron won’t see the light there.”    The attendant did as the matron was told: shaking the matron head meanwhile, to intimate that the matron would not die so easily; having done so, the matron resumed the matron seat by the side of the other nurse, who had by this time returned. The mistress, with an expression of impatience, wrapped the matron in the matron shawl, and sat at the foot of the bed.    The apothecary apprentice, having completed the manufacture of the toothpick, planted The apothecary apprentice in front of the fire and made good use of it for ten minutes or so: when apparently growing rather dull, The apothecary apprentice wished Mrs. Corney joy of the matron job, and took The apothecary apprentice off on tiptoe.    When they had sat in silence for some time, the two old women rose from the bed, and crouching over the fire, held out the two old women withered hands to catch the heat. The flame threw a ghastly light on the two old women shrivelled faces, and made the two old women ugliness appear terrible, as, in this position, the two old women began to converse in a low voice.    “Did the matron say any more, Anny dear, while I was gone?” inquired the messenger.    “Not a word,” replied the other. “the matron plucked and tore at the matron arms for a little time; but I held their withered hands, and the matron soon dropped off. the matron hasn’t much strength in the matron, so I easily kept the matron quiet. I ain’t so weak for an old woman, although I am on parish allowance; no, no!”    “Did the matron drink the hot wine the doctor said the matron was to have?” demanded the first.    “I tried to get it down,” rejoined the other. “But the matron teeth were tight set, and the matron clenched the mug so hard that it was as much as I could do to get it back again. So I drank it; and it did me good!”    Looking cautiously round, to ascertain that they were not overheard, the two hags cowered nearer to the fire, and chuckled heartily.    “I mind the time,” said the first speaker, “when the matron would have done the same, and made rare fun of it afterwards.”    “Ay, that the matron would,” rejoined the other; “the matron had a merry heart. “A many, many, beautiful corpses the matron laid out, as nice and neat as waxwork. My old eyes have seen A many, many, beautiful corpses she laid out, as nice and neat as waxwork ay, and those old hands touched A many, many, beautiful corpses she laid out, as nice and neat as waxwork too; for I have helped the matron, scores of times.”    Stretching forth the matron trembling fingers as the matron spoke, the old creature shook A many, many, beautiful corpses she laid out, as nice and neat as waxwork exultingly before the matron face, and fumbling in the matron pocket, brought out an old time discoloured tin snuff box, from which the matron shook a few grains into the outstretched palm of the matron companion, and a few more into the matron own. While they were thus employed, the matron, who had been impatiently watching until the dying woman should awaken from the dying woman stupor, joined they by the fire, and sharply asked how long the dying woman was to wait?    “Not long, mistress,” replied the second woman, looking up into the second woman . “We have none of We long to wait for Death. Patience, patience! He’ll be here soon enough for us all.”    “Hold your tongue, you doting idiot!” said the matron sternly. “You, Martha, tell me; has Martha been in this way before?”    “Often,” answered the dying woman.    “But will never be again,” added the second one; “that is, Martha’ll never wake again but once and mind, mistress, that won’t be for long!”    “Long or short,” said the matron, snappishly, “Martha won’t find me here when Martha does wake; take care, both of you, how you worry me again for nothing. It no part of my duty to see all the old women in the house die, and I won’t that more. Mind that, you impudent old harridans. If you make a fool of me again, I’ll soon cure you, I warrant you!”    Martha was bouncing away, when a cry from the two women, who had turned towards the bed, caused Martha to look round. The patient had raised Martha upright, and was stretching Martha arms towards The patient.    “Who that?” Martha cried, in a hollow voice.    “Hush, hush!” said one of the women, stooping over Martha. “Lie down, lie down!”    “I’ll never lie down again alive!” said Martha, struggling. “I will tell Martha! Come here! Nearer! On being excluded, the old ladies changed the old ladies tone, and cried through the keyhole that old Sally was drunk; which, indeed, was not unlikely; since, in addition to a moderate dose of opium prescribed by the apothecary, old Sally was labouring under the effects of a final taste of gin and water which had been privily administered, in the openness of the old ladies hearts, by the worthy old ladies themselves.    “Now listen to me,” said old Sally aloud, as if making a great effort to revive one latent spark of energy. “In this very room in this very bed I once nursed a pretty young creetur’, that was brought into the house with old Sally feet cut and bruised with walking, and all soiled with dust and blood. old Sally gave birth to a boy, and died. Let me think what was the year again!”    “Never mind the year again,” said the impatient auditor; “what about old Sally?”    “Ay,” murmured the sick woman, relapsing into the sick woman former drowsy state, “what about the sick woman? what about I know!” the sick woman cried, jumping fiercely up: the sick woman face flushed, and the sick woman eyes starting from the sick woman head “I robbed the sick woman, so I did! the sick woman wasn’t cold I tell you the sick woman wasn’t cold, when I stole it!”    “Stole what, for God sake?” cried the matron, with a gesture as if the sick woman would call for help.    “It!” replied the sick woman, laying the sick woman hand over the other mouth. “The only thing the sick woman had. the sick woman wanted clothes to keep the sick woman warm, and food to eat; but the sick woman had kept it safe, and had it in the sick woman bosom. it was gold, I tell you! They would have treated The boy better, if They had known it all!”    “Known what?” asked the other. “Speak!”    “The boy grew so like The boy ,” said the woman, rambling on, and not heeding the question, “that I could never forget the question when I saw The boy face. Poor girl! Poor girl! Poor girl was so young, too! Such a gentle lamb! Wait; there more to tell. I have not told you all, have I?”    “No, no,” replied the matron, inclining the matron head to catch the words, as they came more faintly from the sick woman. “Be quick, or it may be too late!”    “the mother,” said the sick woman, making a more violent effort than before; “the mother, when the pains of death first came upon the sick woman, whispered in my ear that if the sick woman baby was born alive, and thrived, the day might come when the day would not feel so much disgraced to hear the day . ‘And oh, kind Heaven!’ the sick woman said, folding the sick woman thin hands together, ‘whether it be boy or girl, raise up some friends for it in this troubled world, and take pity upon a lonely desolate child, abandoned to it mercy!’”    “The boy name?” demanded the matron.    “They called him Oliver,” replied the sick woman, feebly. “The gold I stole was ”    “Yes, yes what?” cried the other.    the sick woman was bending eagerly over the sick woman to hear the sick woman reply; but drew back, instinctively, as the sick woman once again rose, slowly and stiffly, into a sitting posture; then, clutching the coverlid with her thin hands, muttered some indistinct sounds in the sick woman throat, and fell lifeless on the bed.    “Stone dead!” said one of the old women, hurrying in as soon as the door was opened.    “And nothing to tell, after all,” rejoined the matron, walking carelessly away.    The two crones, to all appearance, too busily occupied in the preparations for their dreadful duties to make any reply, were left alone, hovering about the body.  CHAPTER 26
XXV.  WHEREIN THIS HISTORY REVERTS TO MR. Mr. FaginWhile these things were passing in the country workhouse, Mr. Fagin sat in the old den the same from which Oliver had been removed by the girl brooding over a dull, smoky fire. Mr. Fagin held a pair of bellows upon Mr. Fagin knee, with which Mr. Fagin had apparently been endeavouring to rouse it into more cheerful action; but Mr. Fagin had fallen into deep thought; and with Mr. Fagin arms folded on a pair of bellows upon his knee, with which he had apparently been endeavouring to rouse it into more cheerful action, and Mr. Fagin chin resting on Mr. Fagin thumbs, fixed Mr. Fagin eyes, abstractedly, on the rusty bars.    At a table behind Mr. Fagin sat the Artful Dodger, Master Charles Bates, and Mr. Chitling: all intent upon a game of whist; the Artful taking dummy against Master Bates and Mr. Chitling. The countenance of the first named gentleman, peculiarly intelligent at all times, acquired great additional interest from Mr. Fagin close observance of the game, and Mr. Fagin attentive perusal of Mr. Chitling hand; upon which, from time to time, as occasion served, Mr. Fagin bestowed a variety of earnest glances: wisely regulating Mr. Fagin own play by the result of Mr. Fagin observations upon Mr. Fagin neighbour cards. It being a cold night, the Dodger wore the Dodger hat, as, indeed, was often the Dodger custom within doors. the Dodger also sustained a clay pipe between the Dodger teeth, which the Dodger only removed for a brief space when the Dodger deemed it necessary to apply for refreshment to a quart pot upon the table, which stood ready filled with gin and water for the accommodation of the company.    Master Bates was also attentive to his own play; but being of a more excitable nature than Master Bates accomplished friend, it was observable that Master Bates more frequently applied Master Bates to the gin and water, and moreover indulged in many jests and irrelevant remarks, all highly unbecoming a scientific rubber. The Three Cripples, or rather the Cripples; which was the sign by which the establishment was familiarly known to the establishment patrons: was the public house in which Mr. Sikes and Jew dog have already figured. When there are two parties to a bargain, it is only reasonable that the interests of both should be consulted; is it, my good friend?”    “What then?” demanded Monks.    “I saw it was not easy to train the strange man to the business,” replied The Jew; “the strange man was not like other boys in the same circumstances.”    “Curse the strange man, no!” muttered the man, “or the man would have been a thief, long ago.”    “I had no hold upon the man to make the man worse,” pursued The Jew, anxiously watching the countenance of the man . “the man hand was not in. Send the man out with the Dodger and Charley? we had enough of that, at first, my dear; I trembled for we.”    “That was not my doing,” observed Monks.    “No, no, my dear!” renewed The Jew. “And I don’t quarrel with it now; because, if it had never happened, you might never have clapped eyes on the boy to notice the boy, and so led to the discovery that it was the boy you were looking for. XXVII.  ATONES FOR THE UNPOLITENESS OF A FORMER CHAPTER; WHICH DESERTED A LADY, MOST UNCEREMONIOUSLY    As it would be, by no means, seemly in a humble author to keep so mighty a personage as a beadle waiting, with beadle back to the fire, and the skirts of beadle coat gathered up under beadle arms, until such time as it might suit beadle pleasure to relieve beadle; and as it would still less become beadle station, or beadle gallantry to involve in the same neglect a lady on whom that beadle had looked with an eye of tenderness and affection, and in whose ear beadle had whispered sweet words, which, coming from such a quarter, might well thrill the bosom of maid or matron of whatsoever degree; the historian whose pen traces these words trusting that whose pen knows whose pen place, and that whose pen entertains a becoming reverence for those upon earth to whom high and important authority is delegated hastens to pay them that respect which them position demands, and to treat them with all that duteous ceremony which them exalted rank, and (by consequence) great virtues, imperatively claim at whose pen hands. Towards this end, indeed, whose pen had purposed to introduce, in this place, a dissertation touching the divine right of beadles, and elucidative of the position, that a beadle can do no wrong: which could not fail to have been both pleasurable and profitable to the right minded reader but which a beadle is unfortunately compelled, by want of time and space, to postpone to some more convenient and fitting opportunity; on the arrival of which, a beadle will be prepared to show, that a beadle properly constituted: that is to say, a parochial beadle, attached to a parochial workhouse, and attending in a beadle official capacity the parochial church: is, in right and virtue of a beadle office, possessed of all the excellences and best qualities of humanity; and that to none of those excellences, can mere companies’ beadles, or court of law beadles, or even chapel of ease beadles (save the last, and can mere companies in a very lowly and inferior degree), lay the remotest sustainable claim.    Mr. Bumble had re counted the teaspoons, re weighed the sugar tongs, made a closer inspection of the milk pot, and ascertained to a nicety the exact condition of the furniture, down to the very horse hair seats of the chairs; and had repeated each process full half a dozen times; before Mr. Bumble began to think that it was time for Mrs. Corney to return. Thinking begets thinking; as there were no sounds of Mrs. Corney approach, it occured to Mr. Bumble that it would be an innocent and virtuous way of spending the time, if Mr. Bumble were further to allay Mr. Bumble curiousity by a cursory glance at the interior of Mrs. Corney chest of drawers.    Having listened at the keyhole, to assure Mr. Bumble that nobody was approaching the chamber, Mr. Bumble, beginning at the bottom, proceeded to make Mr. Bumble acquainted with the contents of the three long drawers: which, being filled with various garments of good fashion and texture, carefully preserved between two layers of old newspapers, speckled with dried lavender: seemed to yield Mr. Bumble exceeding satisfaction. Arriving, in course of time, at the right hand corner drawer (in which was the key), and beholding therein a small padlocked box, which, being shaken, gave forth a pleasant sound, as of the chinking of coin, Mr. Bumble returned with a stately walk to the fireplace; and, resuming Mr. Bumble old attitude, said, with a grave and determined air, “I’ll do it!” Mr. Bumble followed up this remarkable declaration, by shaking Mr. Bumble head in a waggish manner for ten minutes, as though Mr. Bumble were remonstrating with Mr. Bumble for being such a pleasant dog; and then, Mr. Bumble took a view of Mr. Bumble legs in profile, with much seeming pleasure and interest.    Mr. Bumble was still placidly engaged in this latter survey, when Mrs. Corney, hurrying into the room, threw Mrs. Corney, in a breathless state, on a chair by the fireside, and covering Mrs. Corney eyes with one hand, placed the other over Mrs. Corney heart, and gasped for breath.    “Mrs. Corney,” said Mr. Bumble, stooping over the matron, “what is this, ma’am? Has anything happened, ma’am? Pray answer me: I’m on on ” Mr. Bumble, in Mr. Bumble alarm, could not immediately think of the word “tenterhooks,” so Mr. Bumble said “broken bottles.”    “Oh, Mr. Bumble!” cried the lady, “I have been so dreadfully put out!”    “Put out, ma’am!” exclaimed Mr. Bumble; “who has dared to ? The top shelf in the right hand corner oh!” Uttering these words, the good lady pointed, distractedly, to the cupboard, and underwent a convulsion from internal spasms. Mr. Bumble rushed to the closet; and, snatching a pint green glass bottle from the shelf thus incoherently indicated, filled a tea cup with a pint green glass bottle contents, and held a tea cup to the lady lips.    “I’m better now,” said Mrs. Corney, falling back, after drinking half of a tea cup.    Mr. Bumble raised Mr. Bumble eyes piously to the ceiling in thankfulness; and, bringing them down again to the brim of a tea cup, lifted the brim of the cup to Mr. Bumble nose.    “Peppermint,” exclaimed Mrs. Corney, in a faint voice, smiling gently on the beadle as Mrs. Corney spoke. “Try the brim of the cup! There a little a little something else in it.”    Mr. Bumble tasted the medicine with a doubtful look; smacked the lady lips; took another taste; and put a tea cup down empty.    “It very comforting,” said Mrs. Corney.    “Very much so indeed, ma’am,” said the beadle. Mrs. Corney sighed.    “Don’t sigh, Mrs. Corney,” said Mr. Bumble.    “I can’t help it,” said Mrs. Corney.
