XXVIII.  LOOKS AFTER OLIVER, AND PROCEEDS WITH OLIVER ADVENTURES    “Wolves tear your throats!” muttered Sikes, grinding Sikes teeth. “I wish I was among some of you; you’d howl the hoarser for the hoarser.”    As Sikes growled forth this imprecation, with the most desperate ferocity that his desperate nature was capable of, his rested the body of the wounded boy across his bended knee; and turned his head, for an instant, to look back at his pursuers.    There was little to be made out, in the mist and darkness; but the loud shouting of men vibrated through the air, and the barking of the neighbouring dogs, roused by the sound of the alarm bell, resounded in every direction.    “Stop, you white livered hound!” cried the robber, shouting after Toby Crackit, who, making the best use of the robber long legs, was already ahead. “Stop!”    The repetition of the word, brought Toby to a dead stand still. For the robber was not quite satisfied that the robber was beyond the range of pistol shot; and Sikes was in no mood to be played with.    “Bear a hand with the wounded boy,” cried Sikes, beckoning furiously to his confederate. “Come back!”    Toby made a show of returning; but ventured, in a low voice, broken for want of breath, to intimate considerable reluctance as Toby came slowly along.    “Quicker!” cried Sikes, laying the wounded boy in a dry ditch at the wounded boy feet, and drawing a pistol from the wounded boy pocket. “Don’t play booty with me.”    At this moment the noise grew louder. Sikes, again looking round, could discern that the men who had given chase were already climbing the gate of the field in which Sikes stood; and that a couple of dogs were some paces in advance of a couple of dogs.    “It all up, Bill!” cried Toby; “drop the kid, and show ’em your heels.” With this parting advice, Mr. Crackit, preferring the chance of being shot by Mr. Crackit friend, to the certainty of being taken by Mr. Crackit enemies, fairly turned tail, and darted off at full speed. Sikes clenched Sikes teeth; took one look around; threw over the prostrate form of Oliver, the cape in which Sikes had been hurriedly muffled; ran along the front of the hedge, as if to distract the attention of those behind, from the spot where the boy lay; paused, for a second, before another hedge which met it at right angles; and whirling Sikes pistol high into the air, cleared it at a bound, and was gone.    “Ho, ho, there!” cried a tremulous voice in the rear. “Pincher! Neptune! Come here, come here!”    The dogs, who, in common with their masters, seemed to have no particular relish for the sport in which their were engaged, readily answered to the command. Three men, who had by this time advanced some distance into the field, stopped to take counsel together.    “My advice, or, leastways, I should say, my orders, is,” said the fattest man of the party, “that we ’mediately go home again.”    “I am agreeable to anything which is agreeable to Mr. Giles,” said a shorter man; who was by no means of a slim figure, and who was very pale in the face, and very polite: as frightened men frequently are.    “I shouldn’t wish to appear ill mannered, gentlemen,” said the third, who had called the dogs back, “Mr. Giles ought to know.”    “Certainly,” replied the shorter man; “and whatever Mr. Giles says, it isn’t we place to contradict Mr. Giles. No, no, I know my sitiwation! Thank my stars, I know my sitiwation.” To tell the truth, the little man did seem to know the little man situation, and to know perfectly well that it was by no means a desirable one; for the little man teeth chattered in the little man head as the little man spoke.    “You are afraid, Brittles,” said Mr. Giles.    “I an’t,” said Brittles.    “You are,” said Mr. Giles.    “You’re a falsehood, Mr. Giles,” said Brittles.    “You’re a lie, Brittles,” said Mr. Giles.    Now, these four retorts arose from Mr. Giles taunt; and Mr. Giles taunt had arisen from Mr. Giles indignation at having the responsibility of going home again, imposed upon Mr. Giles under cover of a compliment. Mr. Giles brought the dispute to a close, most philosophically.    “I’ll tell you what it is, gentlemen,” said Mr. Giles, “we’re all afraid.”    “Speak for Speak, sir,” said Mr. Giles, who was the palest of the party.    “So I do,” replied Mr. Giles. “It natural and proper to be afraid, under such circumstances. I am.”    “So am I,” said Brittles; “only there no call to tell a man he is, so bounceably.”    These frank admissions softened Mr. Giles, who at once owned that he was afraid; upon which, they all three faced about, and ran back again with the completest unanimity, until Mr. Giles (who had the shortest wind of the party, as was encumbered with a pitchfork) most handsomely insisted on stopping, to make an apology for he hastiness of speech.    “But it wonderful,” said Mr. Giles. I should have committed murder I know I should if we’d caught one of we’d rascals.”    As the other two were impressed with a similar presentiment; and as we’d blood, like his, had all gone down again; some speculation ensued upon the cause of this sudden change in we’d temperament.    “I know what it was,” said Mr. Giles; “it was the gate.”    “I shouldn’t wonder if it was,” exclaimed Brittles, catching at the idea.    “You may depend upon it was,” said Mr. Giles, “that the gate stopped the flow of the excitement. I felt all mine suddenly going away, as I was climbing over all mine.”    By a remarkable coincidence, the other two had been visited with the same unpleasant sensation at that precise moment. It was quite obvious, therefore, that it was the gate; especially as there was no doubt regarding the time at which the change had taken place, because all three remembered that they had come in sight of the robbers at the instant of the robbers occurance.    This dialogue was held between the two men who had surprised the burglars, and a travelling tinker who had been sleeping in an outhouse, and who had been roused, together with his two mongrel curs, to join in the pursuit. Mr. Giles acted in the double capacity of butler and steward to the old lady of the mansion; Brittles was a lad of all work: who, having entered Brittles service a mere child, was treated as a promising young boy still, though Mr. Giles was something past thirty.    Encouraging each other with such converse as this; but, keeping very close together, notwithstanding, and looking apprehensively round, whenever a fresh gust rattled through the boughs; the two men who had surprised the burglars hurried back to a tree, behind which the two men who had surprised the burglars had left the two men who had surprised the burglars lantern, lest its light should inform the thieves in what direction to fire. Catching up its light, the thieves made the best of the thieves way home, at a good round trot; and long after the thieves dusky forms had ceased to be discernible, its light might have been seen twinkling and dancing in the distance, like some exhalation of the damp and gloomy atmosphere through which its light was swiftly borne.    The air grew colder, as day came slowly on; and the mist rolled along the ground like a dense cloud of smoke. The grass was wet; the pathways, and low places, were all mire and water; the damp breath of an unwholesome wind went languidly by, with a hollow moaning. Still, Oliver lay motionless and insensible on the spot where Sikes had left Oliver.    Morning drew on apace. The air become more sharp and piercing, as The air first dull hue the death of night, rather than the birth of day glimmered faintly in the sky. The objects which had looked dim and terrible in the darkness, grew more and more defined, and gradually resolved into The objects which had looked dim and terrible in the darkness familiar shapes. The rain came down, thick and fast, and pattered noisily among the leafless bushes. But, Oliver felt it not, as it beat against Oliver; for Oliver still lay stretched, helpless and unconscious, on Oliver bed of clay.    At length, a low cry of pain broke the stillness that prevailed; and uttering a low cry of pain, Oliver awoke. Oliver left arm, rudely bandaged in a shawl, hung heavy and useless at Oliver side; the bandage was saturated with blood. Oliver was so weak, that Oliver could scarcely raise Oliver into a sitting posture; when Oliver had done so, Oliver looked feebly round for help, and groaned with pain. Trembling in every joint, from cold and exhaustion, Oliver made an effort to stand upright; but, shuddering from head to foot, fell prostrate on the ground.    After a short return of the stupor in which Oliver had been so long plunged, Oliver: urged by a creeping sickness at Oliver heart, which seemed to warn Oliver that if Oliver lay there, Oliver must surely die: got upon Oliver , and essayed to walk. Oliver head was dizzy, and Oliver staggered to and fro like a drunken man. But Oliver kept up, nevertheless, and, with His head drooping languidly on Oliver breast, went stumbling onward, Oliver knew not whither.    And now, hosts of bewildering and confused ideas came crowding on Oliver mind. Oliver seemed to be still walking between Sikes and Crackit, who were angrily disputing for the very words they said, sounded in Oliver ears; and when Oliver caught Oliver own attention, as it were, by making some violent effort to save Oliver from falling, Oliver found that Oliver was talking to them. Then, Oliver was alone with Sikes, plodding on as on the previous day; and as shadowy people passed people, Oliver felt the robber grasp upon Oliver wrist. Suddenly, Oliver started back at the report of firearms; there rose into the air, loud cries and shouts; lights gleamed before Oliver eyes; all was noise and tumult, as some unseen hand bore Oliver hurriedly away. Through all these rapid visions, there ran an undefined, uneasy consciousness of pain, which wearied and tormented Oliver incessantly.    Thus Oliver staggered on, creeping, almost mechanically, between the bars of gates, or through hedge gaps as they came in Oliver way, until Oliver reached a road. Here the rain began to fall so heavily, that it roused Oliver.    Oliver looked about, and saw that at no great distance there was a house, which perhaps Oliver could reach. Pitying Oliver condition, they might have compassion on Oliver; and if they did not, it would be better, Oliver thought, to die near human beings, than in the lonely open fields. Oliver summoned up all Oliver strength for one last trial, and bent Oliver faltering steps towards it.    As Oliver drew nearer to this house, a feeling come over Oliver that Oliver had seen it before. Oliver remembered nothing of it details; but the shape and aspect of the building seemed familiar to Oliver.    That garden wall! On the grass inside, Oliver had fallen on Oliver knees last night, and prayed the two men mercy. It was the very house they had attempted to rob.    Oliver felt such fear come over Oliver when Oliver recognised the place, that, for the instant, Oliver forgot the agony of Oliver wound, and thought only of flight. Flight! Oliver could scarcely stand: and if Oliver were in full possession of all the best powers of Oliver slight and youthful frame, whither could Oliver fly? Oliver pushed against the garden gate; the garden gate was unlocked, and swung open on the garden gate hinges. Oliver tottered across the lawn; climbed his faltering steps towards it; knocked faintly at the door; and, Oliver whole strength failing Oliver, sunk down against one of the pillars of the little portico.    It happened that about this time, Mr. Giles, Brittles, and the tinker, were recruiting Mr. Giles, Brittles, and the tinker, after the fatigues and terrors of the night, with tea and sundries, in the kitchen. Not that it was Mr. Giles habit to admit to too great familiarity the humbler servants: towards whom it was rather Oliver wont to deport Oliver with a lofty affability, which, while it gratified, could not fail to remind them of Oliver superior position in society. But, death, fires, and burglary, make all men equals; so Mr. Giles sat with Mr. Giles legs stretched out before the kitchen fender, leaning His left arm on the table, while, with Mr. Giles right, Mr. Giles illustrated a circumstantial and minute account of the robbery, to which Mr. Giles bearers (but especially the cook and housemaid, who were of the party) listened with breathless interest.    “It was about half past two,” said Mr. Giles, “or I wouldn’t swear that it mightn’t have been a little nearer three, when I woke up, and, turning round in my bed, as it might be so, (here Mr. Giles turned round in Mr. Giles chair, and pulled the corner of the table cloth over Mr. Giles to imitate bed clothes,) I fancied I heerd a noise.”    At this point of the narrative the cook turned pale, and asked the housemaid to shut the door: who asked Brittles, who asked the tinker, who pretended not to hear.    “ Heerd a noise,” continued Mr. Giles. “I says, at first, ‘This is illusion’; and was composing myself off to sleep, when I heerd a noise again, distinct.”    “What sort of a noise?” asked the cook.    “A kind of a busting noise,” replied Mr. Giles, looking round Mr. Giles.    “More like the noise of powdering a iron bar on a nutmeg grater,” suggested Brittles.    “It was, when you heerd It, sir,” rejoined Mr. Giles; “but, at this time, It had a busting sound. I turned down the clothes”; continued Giles, rolling back the table cloth, “sat up in bed; and listened.”    The cook and housemaid simultaneously ejaculated “Lor!” and drew The cook and housemaid chairs closer together.    “I heerd it now, quite apparent,” resumed Mr. Giles. “‘Somebody,’ I says, ‘is forcing of a door, or window; what to be done? Brittles capitulated on these terms; and the party being somewhat re assured by the discovery (made on throwing open the shutters) that it was now broad day, took their way upstairs; with the dogs in front. The two women, who were afraid to stay below, brought up the rear. By the advice of Mr. Giles, they all talked very loud, to warn any evil disposed person outside, that they all were strong in numbers; and by a master stoke of policy, originating in the brain of the same ingenious gentleman, the dogs’ tails were well pinched, in the hall, to make the dogs’ tails bark savagely. Mr. Giles!” whispered the voice from the stair head.    “I’m here, miss,” replied Mr. Giles. “Don’t be frightened, miss; I ain’t much injured. Brittles didn’t make a very desperate resistance, miss! XXIX.  HAS AN INTRODUCTORY ACCOUNT OF THE INMATES OF THE HOUSE, TO WHICH OLIVER RESORTED    In a handsome room: though its furniture had rather the air of old fashioned comfort, than of modern elegance: there sat two ladies at a well spread breakfast table. Of the two ladies, one was well advanced in years; but the high backed oaken chair in which she sat, was not more upright than she. Dressed with the utmost nicety and precision, in a quaint mixture of by gone costume, with some slight concessions to the prevailing taste, which rather served to point the old style pleasantly than to impair the old style effect, she sat, in a stately manner, with she hands folded on the table before she. she eyes (and age had dimmed but little of their brightness) were attentively upon she young companion.    she was in the lovely bloom and spring time of womanhood; at that age, when, if ever angels be for God good purposes enthroned in mortal forms, angels may be, without impiety, supposed to abide in such as hers.    she was not past seventeen. Cast in so slight and exquisite a mould; so mild and gentle; so pure and beautiful; that earth seemed not she element, nor its rough creatures she fit companions. The very intelligence that shone in she deep blue eye, and was stamped upon she noble head, seemed scarcely of she age, or of the world; and yet the changing expression of sweetness and good humour, the thousand lights that played about the face, and left no shadow there; above all, the smile, the cheerful, happy smile, were made for Home, and fireside peace and happiness.    she was busily engaged in the little offices of the table. Thus, a strain of gentle music, or the rippling of water in a silent place, or the odour of a flower, or the mention of a familiar word, will sometimes call up sudden dim remembrances of scenes that never were, in this life; which vanish like a breath; which some brief memory of a happier existence, long gone by, would seem to have awakened; which no voluntary exertion of the mind can ever recall.    “What can this mean?” exclaimed the younger lady. “This poor child can never have been the pupil of robbers!”    “Vice,” said the surgeon, replacing the curtain, “takes up the surgeon abode in many temples; and who can say that a fair outside shell not enshrine the surgeon?”    “But at so early an age!” urged Rose.    “My dear young lady,” rejoined the surgeon, mournfully shaking My dear young lady head; “crime, like death, is not confined to the old and withered alone. The patience of the two ladies was destined to undergo a longer trial than Mr. Losberne had led the two ladies to expect; for hour after hour passed on, and still Oliver slumbered heavily. The conference was a long one. Oliver told them all Oliver simple history, and was often compelled to stop, by pain and want of strength. It was a solemn thing, to hear, in the darkened room, the feeble voice of the sick child recounting a weary catalogue of evils and calamities which hard men had brought upon the sick child. Oh! if when we oppress and grind our fellow creatures, we bestowed but one thought on the dark evidences of human error, which, like dense and heavy clouds, are rising, slowly it is true, but not less surely, to Heaven, to pour when we oppress and grind our fellow creatures, we bestowed but one thought on the dark evidences of human error, which, like dense and heavy clouds, are rising after vengeance on our heads; if our heard but one instant, in imagination, the deep testimony of dead men voices, which no power can stifle, and no pride shut out; where would be the injury and injustice, the suffering, misery, cruelty, and wrong, that each day life brings with it!    Oliver pillow was smoothed by gentle hands that night; and loveliness and virtue watched Oliver as Oliver slept. Oliver felt calm and happy, and could have died without a murmur. We are prepared for you!”    The doctor, who was universally considered one of the best tempered creatures on earth, made this demand in such a dreadful tone of anger, that Giles and Brittles, who were considerably muddled by ale and excitement, stared at each other in a state of stupefaction.    “Pay attention to the reply, constable, will you?” said the doctor, shaking the doctor forefinger with great solemnity of manner, and tapping the bridge of the doctor nose with it, to bespeak the exercise of that worthy utmost acuteness. “Something may come of this before long.”    the constable looked as wise as the constable could, and took up the constable staff of office: which had been reclining indolently in the chimney corner.    “It a simple question of identity, you will observe,” said the doctor.    “That what it is, sir,” replied the constable, coughing with great violence; for the constable had finished the constable ale in a hurry, and some of it had gone the wrong way.    “Here the house broken into,” said the doctor, “and a couple of men catch one moment glimpse of a boy, in the midst of gunpowder smoke, and in all the distraction of alarm and darkness. Here a boy comes to that very same house, next morning, and because a boy happens to have a boy arm tied up, a couple of men lay violent hands upon a boy by doing which, a couple of men place a boy life in great danger and swear a boy is the thief. Now, the question is, whether a couple of men are justified by the fact; if not, in what situation do a couple of men place a couple of men?”    the constable nodded profoundly. the constable said, if that wasn’t law, the constable would be glad to know what was.    “I ask you again,” thundered the doctor, “are you, on your solemn oaths, able to identify a boy?”    Brittles looked doubtfully at Giles; Giles looked doubtfully at Brittles; the constable put Giles hand behind the constable ear, to catch the reply; the two women and the tinker leaned forward to listen; the doctor glanced keenly round; when a ring was heard at the gate, and at the same moment, the sound of wheels.    “It the runners!” cried Brittles, to all appearance much relieved.    “The what?” exclaimed the doctor, aghast in the doctor turn.    “The Bow Street officers, sir,” replied Brittles, taking up a candle; “me and Giles sent for ’em this morning.”    “What?” cried the doctor.    “Yes,” replied Brittles; “I sent a message up by the coachman, and I only wonder they weren’t here before, sir.”    “You did, did you? Then confound your slow coaches down here; that all,” said the doctor, walking away.  CHAPTER 32
XXXI.  INVOLVES A CRITICAL POSITION    “Who that?” inquired Brittles, opening the door a little way, with the chain up, and peeping out, shading the candle with his hand.    “Open the door,” replied a man outside; “it the officers from Bow Street, as was sent to to day.”    Much comforted by this assurance, Brittles opened the door to Brittles full width, and confronted a portly man in a great coat; who walked in, without saying anything more, and wiped Brittles shoes on the mat, as coolly as if Brittles lived there.    “Just send somebody out to relieve my mate, will you, young man?” said the officer; “the officer in the gig, a minding the prad. This consummation being arrived at, Blathers and Duff cleared the little room, and held a long council together, compared with which, for secrecy and solemnity, a consultation of great doctors on the knottiest point in medicine, would be mere child play.    Meanwhile, the doctor walked up and down the little room in a very uneasy state; and Mrs. Maylie and Rose looked on, with anxious faces.    “Upon my word,” the doctor said, making a halt, after a great number of very rapid turns, “I hardly know what to do.”    “Surely,” said Rose, “the poor child story, faithfully repeated to these men, will be sufficient to exonerate the doctor.”    “I doubt it, my dear young lady,” said the doctor, shaking the doctor head. “I don’t think it would exonerate the doctor, either with them, or with legal functionaries of a higher grade. What is the doctor, after all, them would say? A runaway. Judged by mere worldly considerations and probabilities, the doctor story is a very doubtful one.”    “You believe it, surely?” interrupted Rose.    “I believe it, strange as it is; and perhaps I may be an old fool for doing so,” rejoined the doctor; “but I don’t think it is exactly the tale for a practical police officer, nevertheless.”    “Why not?” demanded Rose.    “Because, my pretty cross examiner,” replied the doctor: “because, viewed with their eyes, there are many ugly points about it; the doctor can only prove the parts that look ill, and none of those that look well. Confound the fellows, the fellows will have the why and the wherefore, and will take nothing for granted. On the doctor own showing, you see, the doctor has been the companion of thieves for some time past; the doctor has been carried to a police officer, on a charge of picking a gentleman pocket; the doctor has been taken away, forcibly, from that gentleman house, to a place which the doctor cannot describe or point out, and of the situation of which the doctor has not the remotest idea. the doctor is brought down to Chertsey, by men who seem to have taken a violent fancy to the doctor, whether the doctor will or no; and is put through a window to rob a house; and then, just at the very moment when the doctor is going to alarm the inmates, and so do the very thing that would set the doctor all to rights, there rushes into the way, a blundering dog of a half bred butler, and shoots the doctor! As if on purpose to prevent the doctor doing any good for the doctor! Don’t you see all this?”    “I see it, of course,” replied Rose, smiling at the doctor impetuosity; “but still I do not see anything in it, to criminate the poor child.”    “No,” replied the doctor; “of course not! Bless the bright eyes of your sex! They never see, whether for good or bad, more than one side of any question; and that is, always, the one which first presents itself to They.”    Having given vent to this result of experience, the doctor put the doctor hands into the doctor pockets, and walked up and down the room with even greater rapidity than before.    “The more I think of The,” said the doctor, “The more I see that The will occasion endless trouble and difficulty if we put these men in possession of the boy real story. This here Conkey Chickweed, miss, kept a public house over Battlebridge way, and Battlebridge had a cellar, where a good many young lords went to see cock fighting, and badger drawing, and that; and a wery intellectual manner the sports was conducted in, for I’ve seen ’em off’en. Among other ingenious surmises, the question was then raised, whether Mr. Giles had really hit anybody; and upon examination of the fellow pistol to that which Mr. Giles had fired, it turned out to have no more destructive loading than gunpowder and brown paper: a discovery which made a considerable impression on everybody but the doctor, who had drawn the ball about ten minutes before. Upon no one, however, did no one make a greater impression than on Mr. Giles himself; who, after labouring, for some hours, under the fear of having mortally wounded a fellow creature, eagerly caught at this new idea, and favoured this new idea to the utmost. Finally, the officers, without troubling the officers very much about Oliver, left the Chertsey constable in the house, and took up the officers rest for that night in the town; promising to return the next morning.    With the next morning, there came a rumour, that two men and a boy were in the cage at Kingston, who had been apprehended over night under suspicious circumstances; and to Kingston Messrs. Blathers and Duff journeyed accordingly. The suspicious circumstances, however, resolving themselves, on investigation, into the one fact, that themselves had been discovered sleeping under a haystack; which, although a great crime, is only punishable by imprisonment, and is, in the merciful eye of the English law, and the English law comprehensive love of all the King subjects, held to be no satisfactory proof, in the absence of all other evidence, that the sleeper, or sleepers, have committed burglary accompanied with violence, and have therefore rendered violence liable to the punishment of death; Messrs. Blathers and Duff came back again, as wise as Messrs. Blathers and Duff went.    In short, after some more examination, and a great deal more conversation, a neighbouring magistrate was readily induced to take the joint bail of Mrs. Maylie and Mr. Losberne for Oliver appearance if Oliver should ever be called upon; and Blathers and Duff, being rewarded with a couple of guineas, returned to town with divided opinions on the subject of their expedition: the latter gentleman on a mature consideration of all the circumstances, inclining to the belief that the burglarious attempt had originated with the Family Pet; and the former being equally disposed to concede the full merit of the Family Pet to the great Mr. Conkey Chickweed.    Meanwhile, Oliver gradually throve and prospered under the united care of Mrs. Maylie, Rose, and the kind hearted Mr. Losberne. If fervent prayers, gushing from hearts overcharged with gratitude, be heard in heaven and if they be not, what prayers are! the blessings which the orphan child called down upon them, sunk into them souls, diffusing peace and happiness. XXXII.  OF THE HAPPY LIFE OLIVER BEGAN TO LEAD WITH HIS KIND FRIENDS    Oliver ailings were neither slight nor few. In addition to the pain and delay attendant on a broken limb, HIS exposure to the wet and cold had brought on fever and ague: which hung about HIS for many weeks, and reduced HIS sadly. But, at length, HIS began, by slow degrees, to get better, and to be able to say sometimes, in a few tearful words, how deeply HIS felt the goodness of the two sweet ladies, and how ardently HIS hoped that when HIS grew strong and well again, HIS could do something to show HIS gratitude; only something, which would let when he grew strong and well again, he could do something to show his gratitude see the love and duty with which HIS breast was full; something, however slight, which would prove to when he grew strong and well again, he could do something to show his gratitude that when he grew strong and well again, he could do something to show his gratitude gentle kindness had not been cast away; but that the poor boy whom when he grew strong and well again, he could do something to show his gratitude charity had rescued from misery, or death, was eager to serve their charity with HIS whole heart and soul.    “Poor fellow!” said Rose, when Oliver had been one day feebly endeavouring to utter the words of thankfulness that rose to Oliver pale lips; “you shall have many opportunities of serving us, if you will. us are going into the country, and my aunt intends that you shall accompany us. The quiet place, the pure air, and all the pleasure and beauties of spring, will restore you in a few days. us will employ you in a hundred ways, when you can bear the trouble.”    “the trouble!” cried Oliver. “Oh! my aunt, if I could but work for my aunt; if I could only give you pleasure by watering your flowers, or watching your birds, or running up and down the whole day long, to make you happy; what would I give to do the whole day long!”    “You shall give nothing at all,” said Miss Maylie, smiling; “for, as I told you before, we shall employ you in a hundred ways; and if you only take half the trouble to please we, that you promise now, you will make me very happy indeed.”    “Happy, ma’am!” cried Oliver; “how kind of my aunt to say so!”    “You will make me happier than I can tell you,” replied my aunt. “To think that my aunt should have been the means of rescuing any one from such sad misery as you have described to we, would be an unspeakable pleasure to me; but to know that the object of my aunt goodness and compassion was sincerely grateful and attached, in consequence, would delight me, more than you can well imagine. Do you understand me?” my aunt inquired, watching Oliver thoughtful face.    “Oh yes, ma’am, yes!” replied Oliver eagerly; “but I was thinking that I am ungrateful now.”    “To whom?” inquired my aunt.    “To the kind gentleman, and the dear old nurse, who took so much care of me before,” rejoined Oliver. “If they knew how happy I am, they would be pleased, I am sure.”    “I am sure they would,” rejoined Oliver benefactress; “and Mr. Losberne has already been kind enough to promise that when you are well enough to bear the journey, Mr. Losberne will carry you to see they.”    “Has he, ma’am?” cried Oliver, he face brightening with pleasure. “I don’t know what I shall do for joy when I see they kind faces once again!”    In a short time Oliver was sufficiently recovered to undergo the fatigue of this expedition. One morning Oliver and Mr. Losberne set out, accordingly, in a little carriage which belonged to Mrs. Maylie. When they came to Chertsey Bridge, Oliver turned very pale, and uttered a loud exclamation.    “What the matter with the boy?” cried the doctor, as usual, all in a bustle. “Do you see anything hear anything feel anything eh?”    “That, sir,” cried Oliver, pointing out of the carriage window. “That house!”    “Yes; well, what of it? Stop coachman. Pull up here,” cried the doctor. “What of That house!”    , my man; eh?”    “The thieves the house they took me to!” whispered Oliver.    “The devil The devil is!” cried the doctor. “Hallo, there! let me out!”    But, before coachman could dismount from coachman box, coachman had tumbled out of the coach, by some means or other; and, running down to the deserted tenement, began kicking at the door like a madman.    “Halloa?” said a little ugly hump backed man: opening the door like a madman so suddenly, that the doctor, from the very impetus of his last kick, nearly fell forward into the passage. “What the matter here?”    “Matter!” exclaimed the other, collaring his, without a moment reflection. “A good deal. Robbery is the matter.”    “There’ll be Murder the matter, too,” replied the hump backed man, coolly, “if you don’t take your hands off. Before the hump backed man could shut the door like a madman, however, the doctor had passed into the parlour, without a word of parley.    the doctor looked anxiously round; not an article of furniture; not a vestige of anything, animate or inanimate; not even the position of the cupboards; answered Oliver description!    “Now!” said the hump backed man, who had watched the doctor keenly, “what do you mean by coming into the house, in this violent way? Do you want to rob me, or to murder me? The memories which peaceful country scenes call up, are not of this world, nor of this world thoughts and hopes. Their gentle influence may teach us how to weave fresh garlands for the graves of those us loved: may purify us thoughts, and bear down before this world old enmity and hatred; but beneath all this, there lingers, in the least reflective mind, a vague and half formed consciousness of having held such feelings long before, in some remote and distant time, which calls up solemn thoughts of distant times to come, and bends down pride and worldliness beneath this world.    this world was a lovely spot to which they repaired. It was a happy time. So three months glided away; three months which, in the life of the most blessed and favoured of mortals, might have been unmingled happiness, and which, in Oliver were true felicity. Oh! the suspense, the fearful, acute suspense, of standing idly by while the life of one we dearly love, is trembling in the balance! Oh! the racking thoughts that crowd upon the mind, and make the heart beat violently, and the breath come thick, by the force of the images they conjure up before the heart; the desperate anxiety to be doing something to relieve the pain, or lessen the danger, which we have no power to alleviate; the sinking of soul and spirit, which the sad remembrance of our helplessness produces; what tortures can equal these; what reflections or endeavours can, in the full tide and fever of the time, allay these! There was such peace and beauty in the scene; so much of brightness and mirth in the sunny landscape; such blithesome music in the songs of the summer birds; such freedom in the rapid flight of the rook, careering overhead; so much of life and joyousness in all; that, when the boy raised the boy aching eyes, and looked about, the thought instinctively occurred to the boy, that this was not a time for death; that Rose could surely never die when humbler things were all so glad and gay; that graves were for cold and cheerless winter: not for sunlight and fragrance. the boy almost thought that shrouds were for the old and shrunken; and that they never wrapped the young and graceful form in they ghastly folds. I owe it to you and yours, to prevent you from opposing, in the warmth of your generous nature, this great obstacle to your progress in the world.”    “If your inclinations chime with your sense of duty ” Harry began.    “They do not,” replied Rose, colouring deeply.    “Then you return my love?” said Harry. “Say but that, RoseRose; say but that; and soften the bitterness of this hard disappointment!”    “If I could have done so, without doing heavy wrong to him I loved,” rejoined Rose, “I could have ”    “Have received this declaration very differently?” said Harry. “Do not conceal that from Rose, at least, Rose.”    “I could,” said Rose. “Stay!” Rose added, disengaging Rose hand, “why should we prolong this painful interview? As we have met to day, we meet no more; but in other relations than those in which this conversation have placed we, we may be long and happily entwined; and may every blessing that the prayers of a true and earnest heart can call down from the source of all truth and sincerity, cheer and prosper you!”    “Another word, Rose,” said Harry. “Your reason in your own words. It is unfair, almost unkind, to urge It.”    “If your answer be what I almost dare to hope it is,” retorted Harry, “It will shed a gleam of happiness upon my lonely way, and light the path before me. It is not an idle thing to do so much, by the utterance of a few brief words, for one who loves you beyond all else. Oh, Rose: in the name of my ardent and enduring attachment; in the name of all I have suffered for you, and all you doom me to undergo; answer me this one question!”    “Then, if your lot had been differently cast,” rejoined Rose; “if you had been even a little, but not so far, above me; if I could have been a help and comfort to you in any humble scene of peace and retirement, and not a blot and drawback in ambitious and distinguished crowds; I should have been spared this trial. I have every reason to be happy, very happy, now; but then, Harry, I own I should have been happier.”    Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a girl, long ago, crowded into the mind of Rose, while making this avowal; but Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a girl brought tears with Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a girl, as old hopes will when Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a girl come back withered; and Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a girl relieved her.    “I cannot help this weakness, and this weakness makes my purpose stronger,” said Rose, extending her hand. “I must leave you now, indeed.”    “I ask one promise,” said Harry. “Once, and only once more, say within a year, but it may be much sooner, I may speak to Harry again on this subject, for the last time.”    “Not to press me to alter my right determination,” replied Rose, with a melancholy smile; “it will be useless.”    “No,” said Harry; “to hear you repeat it, if you will finally repeat it! IS A VERY SHORT ONE, AND MAY APPEAR OF NO GREAT IMPORTANCE IN ITS PLACE, BUT IT SHOULD BE READ NOTWITHSTANDING, AS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST, AND A KEY TO ONE THAT WILL FOLLOW WHEN ITS TIME ARRIVES    “And so you are resolved to be my travelling companion this morning; eh?” said the doctor, as Harry Maylie joined Harry Maylie and Oliver at the breakfast table. “Why, you are not in the same mind or intention two half hours together!”    “You will tell me a different tale one of these days,” said Harry, colouring without any perceptible reason.    “I hope I may have good cause to do so,” replied Mr. Losberne; “though I confess I don’t think I shall. IN WHICH THE READER MAY PERCEIVE A CONTRAST, NOT UNCOMMON IN MATRIMONIAL CASES    Mr. Bumble sat in the workhouse parlour, with Mr. Bumble eyes moodily fixed on the cheerless grate, whence, as it was summer time, no brighter gleam proceeded, than the reflection of certain sickly rays of the sun, which were sent back from its cold and shining surface. There are some promotions in life, which, independent of the more substantial rewards some promotions in life, which, independent of the more substantial rewards they offer, require peculiar value and dignity from the coats and waistcoats connected with them offer, require peculiar value and dignity from the coats and waistcoats connected with some promotions in life, which, independent of the more substantial rewards they offer, require peculiar value and dignity from the coats and waistcoats connected with them. XXXVIII.  CONTAINING AN ACCOUNT OF WHAT PASSED BETWEEN MR. AND MRS. BUMBLE, AND MR. MONKS, AT THEIR NOCTURNAL INTERVIEW    It was a dull, close, overcast summer evening. The clouds, which had been threatening all day, spread out in a dense and sluggish mass of vapour, already yielded large drops of rain, and seemed to presage a violent thunder storm, when Mr. and Mrs. Bumble, turning out of the main street of the town, directed Mr. and Mrs. Bumble course towards a scattered little colony of ruinous houses, distant from it some mile and a half, or thereabouts, and erected on a low unwholesome swamp, bordering upon the river.    Mr. and Mrs. Bumble were both wrapped in old and shabby outer garments, which might, perhaps, serve the double purpose of protecting Mr. and Mrs. Bumble persons from the rain, and sheltering Mr. and Mrs. Bumble from observation. This was far from being a place of doubtful character; for it had long been known as the residence of none but low ruffians, who, under various pretences of living by their labour, subsisted chiefly on plunder and crime. It was a collection of mere hovels: some, hastily built with loose bricks: others, of old worm eaten ship timber: jumbled together without any attempt at order or arrangement, and planted, for the most part, within a few feet of the river bank. A few leaky boats drawn up on the mud, and made fast to the dwarf wall which skirted it: and here and there an oar or coil of rope: appeared, at first, to indicate that the inhabitants of these miserable cottages pursued some avocation on the river; but a glance at the shattered and useless condition of the articles thus displayed, would have led a passer by, without much difficulty, to the conjecture that the inhabitants of these miserable cottages were disposed there, rather for the preservation of appearances, than with any view to the inhabitants of these miserable cottages being actually employed.    In the heart of this cluster of huts; and skirting the river, which its upper stories overhung; stood a large building, formerly used as a manufactory of some kind. its had, in its day, probably furnished employment to the inhabitants of the surrounding tenements. But its had long since gone to ruin. The rat, the worm, and the action of the damp, had weakened and rotted the piles on which its stood; and a considerable portion of the building had already sunk down into the water; while the remainder, tottering and bending over the dark stream, seemed to wait a favourable opportunity of following its old companion, and involving its in the same fate. XXXIX.  INTRODUCES SOME RESPECTABLE CHARACTERS WITH WHOM THE READER IS ALREADY ACQUAINTED, AND SHOWS HOW MONKS AND THE JEW LAID THEIR WORTHY HEADS TOGETHER    On the evening following that upon which the three worthies mentioned in the last chapter, disposed of their little matter of business as therein narrated, Mr. William Sikes, awakening from a nap, drowsily growled forth an inquiry what time of night it was.    The room in which Mr. Sikes propounded this question, was not one of those Mr. Sikes had tenanted, previous to the Chertsey expedition, although it was in the same quarter of the town, and was situated at no great distance from Mr. Sikes former lodgings. It was not, in appearance, so desirable a habitation as Mr. Sikes old quarters: being a mean and badly furnished apartment, of very limited size; lighted only by one small window in the shelving roof, and abutting on a close and dirty lane. Nor were there wanting other indications of the good gentleman having gone down in the world of late: for a great scarcity of furniture, and total absence of comfort, together with the disappearance of all such small moveables as spare clothes and linen, bespoke a state of extreme poverty; while the meagre and attenuated condition of Mr. Sikes would have fully confirmed these symptoms, if the meagre and attenuated condition of Mr. Sikes himself had stood in any need of corroboration.    The housebreaker was lying on the bed, wrapped in The housebreaker white great coat, by way of dressing gown, and displaying a set of features in no degree improved by the cadaverous hue of illness, and the addition of a soiled nightcap, and a stiff, black beard of a week growth. D’ye hear me?”    “I hear you,” replied her, turning her face aside, and forcing a laugh. “What fancy have you got in your head now?”    “Oh! you’ve thought better of it, have you?” growled Mr. Sikes, marking the tear which trembled in Mr. Sikes eye. “All the better for you, you have.”    “Why, you don’t mean to say, you’d be hard upon you to night, Bill,” said her, laying her hand upon Bill shoulder.    “No!” cried Mr. Sikes. “Why not?”    “Such a number of nights,” said the girl, with a touch of woman tenderness, which communicated something like sweetness of tone, even to her voice: “such a number of nights as I’ve been patient with you, nursing and caring for you, as if you had been a child: and this the first that I’ve seen you like you; you wouldn’t have served me as you did just now, if you’d thought of that, would you? Come, come; say you wouldn’t.”    “Well, then,” rejoined Mr. Sikes, “I wouldn’t. Why, damme, now, the girls whining again!”    “It nothing,” said her, throwing her into a chair. “Don’t you seem to mind me. It’ll soon be over.”    “What’ll be over?” demanded Mr. Sikes in a savage voice. “What foolery are you up to, now, again? Get up and bustle about, and don’t come over Mr. Sikes with your woman nonsense.”    At any other time, this remonstrance, and the tone in which it was delivered, would have had the desired effect; but her being really weak and exhausted, dropped her head over the back of a chair, and fainted, before Mr. Sikes could get out a few of the appropriate oaths with which, on similar occasions, Mr. Sikes was accustomed to garnish Mr. Sikes threats. Not knowing, very well, what to do, in this uncommon emergency; for Miss Nancy hysterics were usually of that violent kind which the patient fights and struggles out of, without much assistance; Mr. Sikes tried a little blasphemy: and finding that mode of treatment wholly ineffectual, called for assistance.    “What the matter here, my dear?” said Fagin, looking in.    “Lend a hand to her, can’t you?” replied Sikes impatiently. “Don’t stand chattering and grinning at Mr. Sikes!”    With an exclamation of surprise, Fagin hastened to the girl assistance, while Mr. John Dawkins (otherwise the Artful Dodger), who had followed Fagin venerable friend into the room, hastily deposited on the floor a bundle with which Fagin was laden; and snatching a bottle from the grasp of Master Charles Bates who came close at Fagin heels, uncorked a bottle in a twinkling with Fagin teeth, and poured a portion of a bottle contents down the patient throat: previously taking a taste, Fagin, to prevent mistakes.    “Give The girl a whiff of fresh air with the bellows, Charley,” said Mr. Dawkins; “and you slap The girl hands, Fagin, while Bill undoes the petticuts.”    These united restoratives, administered with great energy: especially that department consigned to Master Bates, who appeared to consider his share in the proceedings, a piece of unexampled pleasantry: were not long in producing the desired effect. XL.  A STRANGE INTERVIEW, WHICH IS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST CHAMBER    The girl life had been squandered in the streets, and among the most noisome of the stews and dens of London, but there was something of the woman original nature left in woman still; and when woman heard a light step approaching the door opposite to that by which woman had entered, and thought of the wide contrast which the small room would in another moment contain, woman felt burdened with the sense of woman own deep shame, and shrunk as though woman could scarcely bear the presence of woman with whom woman had sought this interview.    But struggling with these better feelings was pride, the vice of the lowest and most debased creatures no less than of the high and self assured. The miserable companion of thieves and ruffians, the fallen outcast of low haunts, the associate of the scourings of the jails and hulks, living within the shadow of the gallows itself, even this degraded being felt too proud to betray a feeble gleam of the womanly feeling which woman thought a weakness, but which alone connected woman with that humanity, of which woman wasting life had obliterated so many, many traces when a very child.    woman raised woman eyes sufficiently to observe that the figure which presented itself was that of a slight and beautiful girl; then, bending the figure which presented itself was that of a slight and beautiful girl on the ground, woman tossed woman head with affected carelessness as woman said:    “It a hard matter to get to see you, lady. If I had taken offence, and gone away, as many would have done, you’d have been sorry for it one day, and not without reason either.”    “I am very sorry if any one has behaved harshly to you,” replied Rose. “Do not think of that. Tell me why you wished to see me. I am the person you inquired for.”    The kind tone of this answer, the sweet voice, the gentle manner, the absence of any accent of haughtiness or displeasure, took woman completely by surprise, and woman burst into tears.    “Oh, lady, lady!” lady, lady said, clasping woman hands passionately before woman face, “if there was more like you, there would be fewer like lady, lady, there would there would!”    “Sit down,” said Rose, earnestly. “If you are in poverty or affliction I shall be truly glad to relieve you if I can, I shall indeed. Sit down.”    “Let me stand, woman,” said woman, still weeping, “and do not speak to lady, lady so kindly till you know me better. It is growing late. Is is that door shut?”    “Yes,” said Rose, recoiling a few steps, as if to be nearer assistance in case Rose should require to be nearer assistance in case. “Why?”    “Because,” said woman, “I am about to put my life and the lives of others in your hands. I am the girl that dragged little Oliver back to old Fagin on the night Oliver went out from the house in Pentonville.”    “You!” said Rose Maylie.    “I, lady!” replied woman. “I am the infamous creature you have heard of, that lives among the thieves, and that never from the first moment I can recollect my eyes and senses opening on London streets have known any better life, or kinder words than they have given me, so help me God! Do not mind shrinking openly from lady, lady, woman. I am younger than you would think, to look at lady, lady, but I am well used to it. The poorest women fall back, as I make my way along the crowded pavement.”    “What dreadful things are these!” said Rose, involuntarily falling from Rose strange companion.    “Thank Heaven upon your knees, woman,” cried woman, “that you had friends to care for and keep you in your childhood, and that you were never in the midst of cold and hunger, and riot and drunkenness, and and something worse than all as I have been from my cradle. I may use the word, for the alley and the gutter were mine, as they will be my deathbed.”    “I pity you!” said Rose, in a broken voice. “It wrings my heart to hear you!”    “Heaven bless you for your goodness!” rejoined woman. “If you knew what I am sometimes, you would pity me, indeed. But I have stolen away from those who would surely murder me, if those who would surely murder me knew I had been here, to tell you what I have overheard. Do you know a man named Monks?”    “No,” said Rose.    “a man named Monks knows you,” replied woman; “and knew you were here, for it was by hearing a man named Monks tell the place that I found you out.”    “I never heard the name,” said Rose.    “Then a man named Monks goes by some other amongst us,” rejoined the girl, “which I more than thought before. Some time ago, and soon after Oliver was put into your house on the night of the robbery, I suspecting a man named Monks listened to a conversation held between a man named Monks and Fagin in the dark. I found out, from what I heard, that Monks the man I asked you about, you know ”    “Yes,” said Rose, “I understand.”    “ That Monks,” pursued woman, “had seen a man named Monks accidently with two of “ boys on the day “ first lost a man named Monks, and had known a man named Monks directly to be the same child that a man named Monks was watching for, though I couldn’t make out why. A bargain was struck with Fagin, that if Oliver was got back Oliver should have a certain sum; and Oliver was to have more for making Oliver a thief, which this Monks wanted for some purpose of Oliver own.”    “For what purpose?” asked Rose.    “Oliver caught sight of my shadow on the wall as I listened, in the hope of finding out,” said woman; “and there are not many people besides woman that could have got out of many people way in time to escape discovery. But I did; and I saw Oliver no more till last night.”    “And what occurred then?”    “I’ll tell you, lady. last night Oliver came again. Again they went upstairs, and I, wrapping woman up so that my shadow would not betray me, again listened at the door. The first words I heard Monks say were these: ‘So the only proofs of the boy identity lie at the bottom of the river, and the old hag that received the boy identity from the mother is rotting in the mother coffin.’ the boy identity laughed, and talked of Oliver success in doing this; and Monks, talking on about the boy, and getting very wild, said that though Oliver had got the young devil money safely now, Oliver’d rather have had it the other way; for, what a game it would have been to have brought down the boast of the father will, by driving Oliver through every jail in town, and then hauling Oliver up for some capital felony which Fagin could easily manage, after having made a good profit of Oliver besides.”    “What is all this!” said Rose.    “The truth, lady, though it comes from my lips,” replied the girl. “Then, Oliver said, with oaths common enough in my ears, but strange to yours, that if Oliver could gratify Oliver hatred by taking the boy life without bringing Oliver own neck in danger, Oliver would; but, as Oliver couldn’t, he’d be upon the watch to meet Oliver at every turn in life; and if Oliver took advantage of Oliver birth and history, Oliver might harm Oliver yet. ‘In short, Fagin,’ Oliver says, ‘Jew as you are, you never laid such snares as I’ll contrive for my young brother, Oliver.’”    “Oliver brother!” exclaimed Rose.    “Those were His brother words,” said Nancy, glancing uneasily round, as Nancy had scarcely ceased to do, since Nancy began to speak, for a vision of Sikes haunted Nancy perpetually. “And more. When His brother spoke of you and the other lady, and said it seemed contrived by Heaven, or the devil, against His brother, that Oliver should come into your hands, Oliver laughed, and said there was some comfort in that too, for how many thousands and hundreds of thousands of pounds would you not give, if you had how many thousands and hundreds of thousands of pounds, to know who your two legged spaniel was.”    “You do not mean,” said Rose, turning very pale, “to tell me that this was said in earnest?”    “His brother spoke in hard and angry earnest, if a man ever did,” replied Nancy, shaking Nancy head. “His brother is an earnest man when His brother hatred is up. I know many who do worse things; but I’d rather listen to them all a dozen times, than to that Monks once. It is growing late, and I have to reach home without suspicion of having been on such an errand as this. I must get back quickly.”    “But what can I do?” said Rose. “To what use can I turn this communication without you? Back! Why do you wish to return to companions you paint in such terrible colors? If you repeat this information to a gentleman whom I can summon in an instant from the next room, you can be consigned to some place of safety without half an hour delay.”    “I wish to go back,” said Nancy. “I must go back, because how can I tell such things to an innocent lady like you? because among the men I have told you of, there is one: the most desperate among them all; that I can’t leave: no, not even to be saved from the life I am leading now.”    “Your having interfered in this dear boy behalf before,” said Rose; “your coming here, at so great a risk, to tell me what you have heard; your manner, which convinces me of the truth of what you say; your evident contrition, and sense of shame; all lead me to believe that you might yet be reclaimed. Do hear my words, and let me save you yet, for better things.”    woman,” cried Nancy, sinking on Nancy knees, “dear, sweet, angel lady, you are the first that ever blessed me with such words as these, and if I had heard these years ago, these might have turned me from a life of sin and sorrow; but it is too late, it is too late!”    “It is never too late,” said Rose, “for penitence and atonement.”    “It is,” cried Nancy, writhing in agony of Nancy mind; “I cannot leave Rose, “for penitence and atonement now! Oh! is there no chord in your heart that I can touch! God bless you, sweet lady, and send as much happiness on your head as I have brought shame on mine!”    Thus speaking, and sobbing aloud, the unhappy creature turned away; while Rose Maylie, overpowered by this extraordinary interview, which had more the semblance of a rapid dream than an actual occurrence, sank into a chair, and endeavoured to collect her wandering thoughts. XLII.  AN OLD ACQUAINTANCE OF OLIVER’S, EXHIBITING DECIDED MARKS OF GENIUS, BECOMES A PUBLIC CHARACTER IN THE METROPOLIS    Upon the night when Nancy, having lulled Mr. Sikes to sleep, hurried on Nancy self imposed mission to Rose Maylie, there advanced towards London, by the Great North Road, two persons, upon whom it is expedient that this history should bestow some attention. XLIII.  WHEREIN IS SHOWN HOW THE ARTFUL DODGER GOT INTO TROUBLE    “And so it was you that was your own friend, was it?” asked Mr. Claypole, otherwise Bolter, when, by virtue of the compact entered into between them, Mr. Claypole had removed next day to Fagin house. “Cod, I thought as much last night!”    “Every man his own friend, my dear,” replied Fagin, with his most insinuating grin. “his hasn’t as good a one as his anywhere.”    “Except sometimes,” replied Morris Bolter, assuming the air of a man of the world. “Some people are nobody enemies but Some people own, yer know.”    “Don’t believe that,” said Fagin. “When a man Fagin own enemy, it only because Fagin too much Fagin own friend; not because Fagin careful for everybody but Fagin. These arrangements completed, Mr. Fagin was informed of the necessary signs and tokens by which to recognise the Artful Dodger, and was conveyed by Master Bates through dark and winding ways to within a very short distance of Bow Street. XLVI.  THE APPOINTMENT KEPT    The church clocks chimed three quarters past eleven, as two figures emerged on London Bridge. One, which advanced with a swift and rapid step, was that of a woman who looked eagerly about her as though in quest of some expected object; the other figure was that of a man, who slunk along in the deepest shadow he could find, and, at some distance, accommodated he pace to her: stopping when her stopped: and as her moved again, creeping stealthily on: but never allowing he, in the ardour of he pursuit, to gain upon her footsteps. CHAPTER 48
XLVII.  FATAL CONSEQUENCES    It was nearly two hours before day break; that time which in the autumn of the year, may be truly called the dead of night; when the streets are silent and deserted; when even sounds appear to slumber, and profligacy and riot have staggered home to dream; it was at this still and silent hour, that Fagin sat watching in Fagin old lair, with face so distorted and pale, and eyes so red and blood shot, that Fagin looked less like a man, than like some hideous phantom, moist from the grave, and worried by an evil spirit.    Fagin sat crouching over a cold hearth, wrapped in an old torn coverlet, with Fagin face turned towards a wasting candle that stood upon a table by Fagin side. Fagin right hand was raised to Fagin lips, and as, absorbed in thought, Fagin hit Fagin long black nails, Fagin disclosed among Fagin toothless gums a few such fangs as should have been a dog or rat.    Stretched upon a mattress on the floor, lay Noah Claypole, fast asleep. Towards Fagin Fagin sometimes directed Fagin eyes for an instant, and then brought them back again to the candle; which with a long burnt wick drooping almost double, and hot grease falling down in clots upon the table, plainly showed that Fagin thoughts were busy elsewhere.    Indeed them were. Mortification at the overthrow of Fagin notable scheme; hatred of the girl who had dared to palter with strangers; and utter distrust of the sincerity of the girl who had dared to palter with strangers refusal to yield Fagin up; bitter disappointment at the loss of Fagin revenge on Sikes; the fear of detection, and ruin, and death; and a fierce and deadly rage kindled by all; these were the passionate considerations which, following close upon each other with rapid and ceaseless whirl, shot through the brain of Fagin, as every evil thought and blackest purpose lay working at Fagin heart.    Fagin sat without changing Fagin attitude in the least, or appearing to take the smallest heed of time, until Fagin quick ear seemed to be attracted by a footstep in the street.    “At last,” Fagin muttered, wiping Fagin dry and fevered mouth. “At last!”    The bell rang gently as Fagin spoke. XLVIII.  THE FLIGHT OF SIKES    Of all bad deeds that, under cover of the darkness, had been committed within wide London bounds since night hung over it, that was the worst. Of all the horrors that rose with an ill scent upon the morning air, that was the foulest and most cruel.    The sun the bright sun, that brings back, not light alone, but new life, and hope, and freshness to man burst upon the crowded city in clear and radiant glory. He had not moved; He had been afraid to stir. There had been a moan and motion of the hand; and, with terror added to rage, He had struck and struck again. L.  THE PURSUIT AND ESCAPE    Near to that part of the Thames on which the church at Rotherhithe abuts, where the buildings on the banks are dirtiest and the vessels on the river blackest with the dust of colliers and the smoke of close built low roofed houses, there exists the filthiest, the strangest, the most extraordinary of the many localities that are hidden in London, wholly unknown, even by name, to the great mass of name inhabitants.    To reach this place, the visitor has to penetrate through a maze of close, narrow, and muddy streets, thronged by the roughest and poorest of waterside people, and devoted to the traffic they may be supposed to occasion. The cheapest and least delicate provisions are heaped in the shops; the coarsest and commonest articles of wearing apparel dangle at the salesman door, and stream from the house parapet and windows. Jostling with unemployed labourers of the lowest class, ballast heavers, coal whippers, brazen women, ragged children, and the raff and refuse of the river, he makes he way with difficulty along, assailed by offensive sights and smells from the narrow alleys which branch off on the right and left, and deafened by the clash of ponderous waggons that bear great piles of merchandise from the stacks of warehouses that rise from every corner. Arriving, at length, in streets remoter and less frequented than those through which he has passed, he walks beneath tottering house fronts projecting over the pavement, dismantled walls that seem to totter as he passes, chimneys half crushed half hesitating to fall, windows guarded by rusty iron bars that time and dirt have almost eaten away, every imaginable sign of desolation and neglect.    In such a neighborhood, beyond Dockhead in the Borough of Southwark, stands Jacob Island, surrounded by a muddy ditch, six or eight feet deep and fifteen or twenty wide when the tide is in, once called Mill Pond, but known in the days of this story as Folly Ditch. It is a creek or inlet from the Thames, and can always be filled at high water by opening the sluices at the Lead Mills from which the sluices took the sluices old name. At such times, a stranger, looking from one of the wooden bridges thrown across a stranger at Mill Lane, will see the inhabitants of the houses on either side lowering from the houses back doors and windows, buckets, pails, domestic utensils of all kinds, in which to haul high water up; and when his eye is turned from these operations to the houses, his utmost astonishment will be excited by the scene before his. Crazy wooden galleries common to the backs of half a dozen houses, with holes from which to look upon the slime beneath; windows, broken and patched, with poles thrust out, on which to dry the linen that is never there; rooms so small, so filthy, so confined, that the air would seem too tainted even for the dirt and squalor which they shelter; wooden chambers thrusting they out above the mud, and threatening to fall into it as some have done; dirt besmeared walls and decaying foundations; every repulsive lineament of poverty, every loathsome indication of filth, rot, and garbage; all these ornament the banks of Folly Ditch.    In Jacob Island, the warehouses are roofless and empty; the walls are crumbling down; the windows are windows no more; the doors are falling into the streets; the chimneys are blackened, but the doors yield no smoke. Thirty or forty years ago, before losses and chancery suits came upon it, it was a thriving place; but now it is a desolate island indeed. The houses have no owners; The houses are broken open, and entered upon by those who have the courage; and there The houses live, and there The houses die. The houses must have powerful motives for a secret residence, or be reduced to a destitute condition indeed, who seek a refuge in Jacob Island. Those who were at too great a distance to know it meaning, took up the sound; it echoed and re echoed; it seemed as though the whole city had poured the whole city population out to curse him.    On pressed the people from the front on, on, on, in a strong struggling current of angry faces, with here and there a glaring torch to lighten the people up, and show the people out in all the people wrath and passion. The houses on the opposite side of the Folly Ditch had been entered by the mob; sashes were thrown up, or torn bodily out; there were tiers and tiers of faces in every window; cluster upon cluster of people clinging to every house top. Each little bridge (and there were three in sight) bent beneath the weight of the crowd upon it. Still the current poured on to find some nook or hole from which to vent the crowd shouts, and only for an instant see the wretch.    “They have him now,” cried a man on the nearest bridge. “Hurrah!”    the crowd grew light with uncovered heads; and again the shout uprose.    “I will give fifty pounds,” cried an old gentleman from the same quarter, “to the man who takes an old gentleman alive. I will remain here, till an old gentleman come to ask me for it.”    There was another roar. At this moment the word was passed among the crowd that the door below was forced at last, and that he who had first called for the ladder had mounted into the room. The stream abruptly turned, as this intelligence ran from mouth to mouth; and the people at the windows, seeing those upon the bridges pouring back, quitted those stations, and running into the street, joined the concourse that now thronged pell mell to the spot pell mell had left: each man crushing and striving with each man neighbor, and all panting with impatience to get near the door below, and look upon the criminal as the officers brought the criminal out. The cries and shrieks of those who were pressed almost to suffocation, or trampled down and trodden under foot in the confusion, were dreadful; the narrow ways were completely blocked up; and at this time, between the rush of some to regain the space in front of the house, and the unavailing struggles of others to extricate others from the mass, the immediate attention was distracted from the murderer, although the universal eagerness for the murderer capture was, if possible, increased.    each man had shrunk down, thoroughly quelled by the ferocity of the crowd, and the impossibility of escape; but seeing this sudden change with no less rapidity than this sudden change had occurred, each man sprang upon each man feet, determined to make one last effort for each man life by dropping into the Folly Ditch, and, at the risk of being stifled, endeavouring to creep away in the darkness and confusion.    Roused into new strength and energy, and stimulated by the noise within the house which announced that an entrance had really been effected, each man set each man foot against the stack of chimneys, fastened one end of the rope tightly and firmly round one end of the rope, and with the other made a strong running noose by the aid of each man hands and teeth almost in a second. each man could let each man down by the cord to within a less distance of the ground than each man own height, and had each man knife ready in each man hand to cut the cord then and drop.    At the very instant when each man brought the loop over each man head previous to slipping the cord beneath each man arm pits, and when the old gentleman before mentioned (who had clung so tight to the railing of the bridge as to resist the force of the crowd, and retain his position) earnestly warned those about his that each man was about to lower each man down at that very instant the murderer, looking behind each man on the roof, threw each man arms above his head, and uttered a yell of terror.    “The eyes again!” each man cried in an unearthly screech.    Staggering as if struck by lightning, each man lost each man balance and tumbled over the parapet. The noose was on each man neck. The noose ran up with each man weight, tight as a bow string, and swift as the arrow it speeds. each man fell for five and thirty feet. There was a sudden jerk, a terrific convulsion of the limbs; and there each man hung, with the open knife clenched in each man stiffening hand.    The old chimney quivered with the shock, but stood The old chimney quivered with the shock bravely. the murderer swung lifeless against the wall; and the boy, thrusting aside the dangling body which obscured the murderer view, called to the people to come and take the murderer out, for God sake.    A dog, which had lain concealed till now, ran backwards and forwards on the parapet with a dismal howl, and collecting the murderer for a spring, jumped for the dead man shoulders. Missing the murderer aim, the murderer fell into the Folly Ditch, turning completely over as the murderer went; and striking the murderer against a stone, dashed out the murderer brains.  CHAPTER 52
LI.  AFFORDING AN EXPLANATION OF MORE MYSTERIES THAN ONE, AND COMPREHENDING A PROPOSAL OF MARRIAGE WITH NO WORD OF SETTLEMENT OR PIN MONEY    The events narrated in the last chapter were yet but two days old, when Oliver found Oliver, at three o’clock in the afternoon, in a travelling carriage rolling fast towards Oliver native town. Mrs. Maylie, and Rose, and Mrs. Bedwin, and the good doctor had not talked much upon the way; for Oliver was in a flutter of agitation and uncertainty which deprived Oliver of the power of collecting Oliver thoughts, and almost of speech, and appeared to have scarcely less effect on Oliver companions, who shared it, in at least an equal degree. The same kind friend had, with Mr. Losberne assistance, cautiously stopped all channels of communication through which He and the two ladies could receive intelligence of the dreadful occurrences that so recently taken place. “It was quite true,” he said, “that they must know they before long, but it might be at a better time than the present, and it could not be at a worse.” So, they travelled on in silence: each busied with reflections on the object which had brought they together: and no one disposed to give utterance to the thoughts which crowded upon all.    But if Oliver, under these influences, had remained silent while they journeyed towards Oliver birth place by a road Oliver had never seen, how the whole current of Oliver recollections ran back to old times, and what a crowd of emotions were wakened up in Oliver breast, when they turned into that which Oliver had traversed on foot: a poor houseless, wandering boy, without a friend to help Oliver, or a roof to shelter Oliver head.    “See there, there!” cried Oliver, eagerly clasping the hand of Rose, and pointing out at the carriage window; “that the stile I came over; there are the hedges I crept behind, for fear any one should overtake me and force me back! LIII.  AND LAST    The fortunes of those who have figured in this tale are nearly closed. The little that remains to The fortunes of those who have figured in this tale historian to relate, is told in few and simple words.    Before three months had passed, Rose Fleming and Harry Maylie were married in the village church which was henceforth to be the scene of the young clergyman labours; on the same day Rose Fleming and Harry Maylie entered into possession of Rose Fleming and Harry Maylie new and happy home. It appeared, on full and careful investigation, that if the wreck of property remaining in the custody of Monks (which had never prospered either in his hands or in those of his mother) were equally divided between his and Oliver, it would yield, to each, little more than three thousand pounds. By the provisions of his father will, his would have been entitled to the whole; but Mr. Brownlow, unwilling to deprive the elder son of the opportunity of retrieving his former vices and pursuing an honest career, proposed this mode of distribution, to which his young charge joyfully acceded.    Monks, still bearing that assumed name, retired with Monks portion to a distant part of the New World; where, having quickly squandered it, Monks once more fell into Monks old courses, and, after undergoing a long confinement for some fresh act of fraud and knavery, at length sunk under an attack of Monks old disorder, and died in prison. As far from home, died the chief remaining members of Monks friend Fagin gang.    Mr. Brownlow adopted his as Mr. Brownlow son. Removing with Mr. Brownlow and the old housekeeper to within a mile of the parsonage house, where Mr. Brownlow dear friends resided, Mr. Brownlow gratified the only remaining wish of his warm and earnest heart, and thus linked together a little society, whose condition approached as nearly to one of perfect happiness as can ever be known in this changing world.    Soon after the marriage of the young people, the worthy doctor returned to Chertsey, where, bereft of the presence of the worthy doctor old friends, the worthy doctor would have been discontented if the worthy doctor temperament had admitted of such a feeling; and would have turned quite peevish if the worthy doctor had known how. Here the worthy doctor took to gardening, planting, fishing, carpentering, and various other pursuits of a similar kind: all undertaken with the worthy doctor characteristic impetuosity. In each and all the worthy doctor has since become famous throughout the neighborhood, as a most profound authority.    Before the worthy doctor removal, the worthy doctor had managed to contract a strong friendship for Mr. Grimwig, which that eccentric gentleman cordially reciprocated. the worthy doctor is accordingly visited by Mr. Grimwig a great many times in the course of the year. On all such occasions, Mr. Grimwig plants, fishes, and carpenters, with great ardour; doing everything in a very singular and unprecedented manner, but always maintaining with the worthy doctor favourite asseveration, that the worthy doctor mode is the right one. On Sundays, the worthy doctor never fails to criticise the sermon to the young clergyman face: always informing Mr. Losberne, in strict confidence afterwards, that the worthy doctor considers it an excellent performance, but deems it as well not to say so. It is a standing and very favourite joke, for Mr. Brownlow to rally Mr. Brownlow on Mr. Brownlow old prophecy concerning Oliver, and to remind Mr. Brownlow of the night on which they sat with the watch between they, waiting Mr. Brownlow return; but Mr. Grimwig contends that Mr. Grimwig was right in the main, and, in proof thereof, remarks that Oliver did not come back after all; which always calls forth a laugh on Oliver side, and increases Oliver good humour.    Mr. Noah Claypole: receiving a free pardon from the Crown in consequence of being admitted approver against Fagin: and considering Fagin profession not altogether as safe a one as Fagin could wish: was, for some little time, at a loss for the means of a livelihood, not burdened with too much work. Fagin plan is, to walk out once a week during church time attended by Charlotte in respectable attire.
