Sometimes Mr. Claypole faints Mr. Claypole, but the result is the same.    Mr. and Mrs. Bumble, deprived of their situations, were gradually reduced to great indigence and misery, and finally became paupers in that very same workhouse in which their had once lorded it over others. Mr. Bumble has been heard to say, that in this reverse and degradation, Mr. Bumble has not even spirits to be thankful for being separated from Mr. Bumble wife.    As to Mr. Giles and Brittles, Mr. Giles and Brittles still remain in Mr. Giles and Brittles old posts, although the former is bald, and the last named boy quite grey. Mr. Giles and Brittles sleep at the parsonage, but divide Mr. Giles and Brittles attentions so equally among the parsonage inmates, and Oliver and Mr. Brownlow, and Mr. Losberne, that to this day the villagers have never been able to discover to which establishment the villagers properly belong.    Master Charles Bates, appalled by Sikes crime, fell into a train of reflection whether an honest life was not, after all, the best. Arriving at the conclusion that it certainly was, Mr. Bumble turned Mr. Bumble back upon the scenes of the past, resolved to amend his back in some new sphere of action. Mr. Bumble struggled hard, and suffered much, for some time; but, having a contented disposition, and a good purpose, succeeded in the end; and, from being a farmer drudge, and a carrier lad, Mr. Bumble is now the merriest young grazier in all Northamptonshire.    And now, the hand that traces these words, falters, as it approaches the conclusion of it task; and would weave, for a little longer space, the thread of these adventures.    I would fain linger yet with a few of those among whom I have so long moved, and share whom happiness by endeavouring to depict it. I would show Rose Maylie in all the bloom and grace of early womanhood, shedding on her secluded path in life soft and gentle light, that fell on all who trod it with her, and shone into all who trod it hearts. I would paint her the life and joy of the fire side circle and the lively summer group; I would follow her through the sultry fields at noon, and hear the low tones of her sweet voice in the moonlit evening walk; I would watch her in all her goodness and charity abroad, and the smiling untiring discharge of domestic duties at home; I would paint her and her dead sister child happy in her and her dead sister child love for one another, and passing whole hours together in picturing the friends whom her and her dead sister child had so sadly lost; I would summon before her, once again, those joyous little faces that clustered round her knee, and listen to her and her dead sister child merry prattle; I would recall the tones of that clear laugh, and conjure up the sympathising tear that glistened in the soft blue eye. These, and a thousand looks and smiles, and turns of thought and speech I would fain recall her and her dead sister child every one.    How Mr. Brownlow went on, from day to day, filling the mind of Mr. Brownlow with stores of knowledge, and becoming attached to Mr. Brownlow, more and more, as Mr. Brownlow nature developed itself, and showed the thriving seeds of all Mr. Brownlow wished Mr. Brownlow to become how Mr. Brownlow traced in Mr. Brownlow new traits of Mr. Brownlow early friend, that awakened in Mr. Brownlow own bosom old remembrances, melancholy and yet sweet and soothing how the two orphans, tried by adversity, remembered itself lessons in mercy to others, and mutual love, and fervent thanks to Mr. Brownlow who had protected and preserved them these are all matters which need not to be told. I have said that these were truly happy; and without strong affection and humanity of heart, and gratitude to that Being whose code is Mercy, and whose great attribute is Benevolence to all things that breathe, happiness can never be attained.    Within the altar of a Church stands a white marble tablet, which bears as yet but one word: “Agnes.” There is no coffin in that tomb; and may no coffin in that tomb be many, many years, before another name is placed above no coffin in that tomb! But, if the spirits of the Dead ever come back to earth, to visit spots hallowed by the love the love beyond the grave of those whom those whom they knew in life knew in life, I believe that the shade of Agnes sometimes hovers round that solemn nook. I believe the shade of Agnes sometimes hovers round that solemn nook none the less because that nook is in a Church, and Agnes was weak and erring.
